[4:0:344,442] Legislative, Judicial, and Executive section of the 

[4:1:344,452] Governor's Budget are either established as 

[4:2:344,462] independent entities under the California 

[5:0:344,472] Constitution, or are departments with a recognized 

[5:1:344,482] need to operate outside of the administrative 

[5:2:344,492] oversight and control of an agency secretary. 

[5:3:344,502] Constitutionally-established bodies include the 

[5:4:344,512] Legislature, Judicial Branch, Governor's Office, 

[5:5:344,522] and constitutional officers. This section also 

[5:6:344,532] includes such independent entities as the 

[6:0:344,541] Inspector General and the California State Lottery.

[1:0:268,43] 0110   Senate 

[1:1:66,64] The Legislature is comprised of two houses, the Senate and the Assembly, and is responsible for enacting laws and 

[1:2:66,74] establishing public policy for California. The State Senate is comprised of 40 members who are elected for 4-year terms. 

[1:3:66,84] Members of the Legislature may serve a maximum of 12 years total in the Senate, the Assembly, or both, in any combination of 

[1:4:66,95] terms. Members first elected before June 6, 2012, however, may generally serve a maximum of 14 years total, with no more 

[1:5:66,105] than two terms in the Senate and three terms in the Assembly.

[0:0:66,13] 2018-19 STATE BUDGET — LJE   10   LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[1:0:139,43] 0150   Contributions to the Legislators' Retirement System 

[1:1:66,64] Established in 1947, the Legislators' Retirement System provides retirement and death benefits for legislators, constitutional 

[1:2:66,74] officers, and legislative statutory officers. 

[2:0:66,95] The Legislators' Retirement System is funded through contributions from the state, members of the system, and investment 

[2:1:66,105] earnings. Between 2000-01 and 2011-12, the Legislators' Retirement System was super-funded and neither the state nor 

[2:2:66,115] members were required to contribute. Beginning in 2012-13, contributions resumed for the state and members. 

[3:0:66,135] The California Public Employees' Retirement System (CalPERS) administers the Legislators' Retirement System on behalf of 

[3:1:66,146] its members. Retirement benefits paid are based upon age, years of service, and final compensation. Benefits for death, 

[3:2:66,156] disability, and surviving dependents of deceased members are also provided under specific conditions. 

[4:0:66,176] Proposition 140, approved by voters in 1990, closed the Legislators' Retirement System to all new legislators after November 

[4:1:66,186] 6, 1990. 

[5:0:66,207] Effective January 1, 2013, the Legislators' Retirement System was closed to all new members by the California Public 

[5:1:66,217] Employees' Pension Reform Act of 2013, Chapter 296, Statutes of 2012 (AB 340). New constitutional officers, statutory 

[5:2:66,227] officers, Sergeant-at-Arms, and the Insurance Commissioner retain optional membership rights in CalPERS.

[1:0:208,43] 0160   Legislative Counsel Bureau 

[2:0:66,64] The Legislative Counsel Bureau (Bureau) provides legal assistance to the two houses of the Legislature, and their members 

[2:1:66,74] and committees, by resolving a large volume of complex legal problems arising in connection with the legislative process. The 

[2:2:66,84] legal services furnished include rendering opinions, drafting bills, counseling, attending as counsel meetings of legislative 

[2:3:66,95] committees, and representing the Legislature in litigation. The attorney-client relationship is maintained and all work is 

[2:4:66,105] confidential. 

[3:0:66,125] In addition, the Bureau prepares and provides necessary indices and appropriate tables necessary to identify legislative 

[3:1:66,135] measures and compiles and indexes statutes and codes. 

[4:0:66,156] The Bureau operates the Legislative Data Center, which provides information technology services in support of the legislative 

[4:1:66,166] information system and the processing of legislative measures. 

[5:0:66,186] Pursuant to Chapter 46 of the Statutes of 2012, the California Commission on Uniform State Laws was transferred into the 

[5:1:66,197] Legislative Counsel Bureau. The California Commission on Uniform State Laws presents to the Legislature uniform laws 

[5:2:66,207] recommended by the National Conference of Commissioners on Uniform State Laws and then promotes passage of these 

[5:3:66,217] uniform acts.

[0:0:66,13] 2018-19 STATE BUDGET — LJE   16   LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[1:0:242,43] 0250   Judicial Branch 

[2:0:66,64] Article VI of the Constitution creates the Supreme Court of California and the Courts of Appeal to exercise the judicial power of 

[2:1:66,74] the state at the appellate level. Article VI also creates the Judicial Council of California to administer the state's judicial system. 

[2:2:66,84] Chapter 869, Statutes of 1997, created the California Habeas Corpus Resource Center to represent any person financially 

[2:3:66,95] unable to employ appellate counsel in capital cases. 

[3:0:66,115] The Lockyer-Isenberg Trial Court Funding Act of 1997 (Chapter 850, Statutes of 1997) provided a stable and consistent 

[4:0:66,125] funding source for the trial courts. Beginning with fiscal year 1997-98, consolidation of the costs of trial court operation was 

[4:1:66,135] implemented at the state level, with the exception of facility, revenue collection, and local judicial benefit costs. This 

[5:0:66,146] implementation capped the counties' general purpose revenue contributions to trial court costs at a revised 1994-95 level. The 

[5:1:66,156] county contributions become part of the Trial Court Trust Fund, which supports all trial court operations. Fine and penalty 

[6:0:66,166] revenue collected by each county is retained or distributed in accordance with statute. Each county makes quarterly payments 

[6:1:66,176] to the Trial Court Trust Fund equal to the fine and penalty revenue received by the state General Fund in 1994-95, as adjusted 

[6:2:66,186] by amounts equivalent to specified fine and fee revenues that counties benefited from in 2003-04. The Trial Court Facilities Act 

[6:3:66,197] of 2002 (Chapter 1082, statutes of 2002) provided a process for the responsibility for court facilities to be transferred from the 

[6:4:66,207] counties to the state by July 1, 2007, which was extended to December 31, 2009, by Chapter 9, Statutes of 2008. The Trial 

[7:0:66,217] Court Facilities Act of 2002 also established several new revenue sources, effective on January 1, 2003. These revenues are 

[7:1:66,227] deposited into the State Court Facilities Construction Fund for the purpose of funding the construction and maintenance of 

[8:0:66,237] court facilities throughout the state. Counties contribute revenues for the ongoing operation and maintenance of court facilities 

[8:1:66,248] based upon historical expenditures for facilities transferred to the state. 

[9:0:66,268] The mission of the Judicial Branch is to resolve disputes arising under the law and to interpret and apply the law consistently, 

[9:1:66,278] impartially, and independently to protect the rights and liberties guaranteed by the Constitutions of California and the United 

[9:2:66,288] States, in a fair, accessible, effective, and efficient manner. 

[10:0:66,309] Because the Judicial Branch's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Judicial Branch has a capital outlay 

[10:1:66,319] program to support this need. For additional information regarding the Judicial Branch's capital outlay program, see 

[10:2:66,329] "Infrastructure Overview."

[11:0:66,303] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES 

[12:0:65,321] •   Support for Trial Court Operations-The Budget includes   $47.8 million General Fund to equalize funding among courts by 

[12:1:76,332] bringing all trial courts up to the statewide average funding level. 

[13:0:65,348] •   Discretionary Funding for Trial Courts-The Budget includes $75 million General Fund for allocation to trial courts statewide 

[13:1:76,358] based on priorities set by the Judicial Council. In approving this augmentation, the Legislature expressed its intent for courts 

[13:2:76,368] to expand court reporter services in family court. 

[14:0:65,384] •   Trial Court Employee Health Benefits and Retirement-The Budget includes an increase of $24.9 million General Fund for trial 

[14:1:76,395] court employee health benefit and retirement   costs. 

[15:0:65,411] •   Trial Court Trust Fund Revenue-The Budget includes an increase of $9 million General Fund, bringing the total to $64 million 

[15:1:76,421] General Fund, to   backfill a further reduction of fine and penalty revenues expected in 2018-19. 

[16:0:65,437] •   Deferred Maintenance-The Budget includes $50 million General Fund   for deferred maintenance projects at trial courts. 

[17:0:65,453] •   Services for Self-Represented Litigants-The Budget includes an increase of $19.1 million General Fund to expand self-help 

[17:1:76,464] services in trial courts to assist the increasing number of self-represented litigants and achieve court efficiencies, bringing 

[17:2:76,474] the   total budget for self-help services to $30.3 million. The Budget also includes $3.2 million General Fund to design, build, 

[17:3:76,484] and maintain a web portal to enable those without legal representation to research, e-file, and track non-criminal cases via 

[17:4:76,494] an online portal. 

[18:0:65,510] •   Language Access-The Budget includes an increase of $4 million General Fund to continue to expand the availability of 

[18:1:76,521] interpreter services in all courts. The Budget also includes $4 million General Fund ongoing to advance implementation of 

[18:2:76,531] the Strategic Plan for Language Access in California Courts by providing funding for language access training for court 

[18:3:76,541] interpreters, and for signage and materials in appropriate languages throughout the courts to enhance effective 

[18:4:76,551] communication for those navigating courthouses. 

[19:0:65,567] •   Pilot Project for Online Adjudication of Minor Traffic Violations-The Budget includes $3.4 million General Fund   to implement 

[19:1:76,578] an eight-court pilot to begin moving toward a civil model for adjudication of minor traffic violations by simplifying current 

[19:2:76,588] criminal procedures. The pilot also includes a tool which will recommend   a reduction to the total amount due and maximum 

[19:3:76,598] monthly payment amounts   for defendants who have established their inability to pay fines and penalties. 

[20:0:65,614] •   Judgeships-The Budget includes $2.9 million General Fund for two additional judgeships in Riverside County, and $1.2 

[20:1:76,624] million General Fund for one additional Justice in the Fourth Appellate District Court of Appeal in the San Bernardino/ 

[20:2:76,635] Riverside area. 

[21:0:65,651] •   Court-Appointed Special Advocate Program-The Budget includes $500,000 General Fund to provide additional grants to 

[21:1:76,661] train and supervise volunteers assigned by a juvenile court judge to children or youth in foster care, leveraging outside 

[21:2:76,671] investments to directly serve approximately 2,200 additional foster youth. 

[22:0:65,687] •   California Courts Protective Order Registry-The Budget includes $200,000 to expand the Registry, which serves as a central 

[22:1:76,698] repository of restraining and protective orders, to the remaining seven trial courts.

[25:1:127,587] display. Because the funding for these expenses is already included in Section 1, they are subtracted from the total "State Trial Court Funding Not Distributed to the Trial Courts" amount used to calculate the total expenditures displayed for "State  

[25:2:127,591] Trial Court Funding Total".  

[26:0:127,603] 3. This reflects judges' compensation paid excluding any amounts related to the reimbursement of courts for payments of judges' compensation as these amounts are already included in the courts' "State Funding" revenue amounts reported in the Trial  

[26:1:127,608] Court Information section ($115.3 million in 2016-17 and $121.2 million in 2017-18). Judges from the Los Angeles, Riverside, and Ventura County Superior Courts are compensated locally and the court/county is reimbursed by the TCTF. In  

[26:2:127,613] addition, 25 courts in 2016-17 and 24 courts in 2017-18 participate in the local salary reimbursement program in which a small portion of the judges' salary is paid locally and reimbursed by the TCTF.
[27:0:127,624] 4. Reflects actual or estimated payments made directly to court-appointed dependency counsel from the TCTF on behalf of superior courts participating in the Dependency Representation, Administration, Funding, and Training (DRAFT) Program.  

[27:1:127,629] Courts not participating in the DRAFT program are reimbursed up to a maximum amount from the TCTF for payments to court-appointed dependency counsel.

[28:1:127,645] the JBWCF, these expenses reflect payments of trial court employee and judge related workers' compensation claims from the JBWCF less the amount transferred from the TCTF.

[0:0:66,24] 2018-19 STATE BUDGET — LJE   22 

[1:0:382,24] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[2:0:207,38] 0250   Judicial Branch - Continued 

[3:0:66,72] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[3:1:66,91] 0130 - SUPREME COURT 

[4:0:66,111] The Supreme Court is the highest court in the California judicial system. Its decisions are binding on all other California state 

[4:1:66,121] courts. The Chief Justice of California and the six Associate Justices entertain petitions seeking review of decisions from the 

[4:2:66,131] Courts of Appeal, original petitions for extraordinary relief (such as writs of mandate or habeas corpus), and recommendations 

[4:3:66,142] for discipline of judicial officers and attorneys. The Court grants review and issues opinions in order to settle legal questions of 

[4:4:66,152] statewide importance. In addition, under the California Constitution, all death penalty judgments are appealed directly to the 

[4:5:66,162] Supreme Court. 

[4:6:66,180] 0135 - COURTS OF APPEAL 

[5:0:66,200] Established by a constitutional amendment in 1904, the Courts of Appeal are California's intermediate courts of review. The six 

[5:1:66,210] District Courts of Appeal hear appeals and original proceedings at nine different locations around the state. Cases before the 

[5:2:66,221] Courts of Appeal involve every area of civil and criminal law. 

[5:3:66,238] 0140 - JUDICIAL COUNCIL 

[6:0:66,259] The Judicial Council of California is the constitutional policy-making body for the state judiciary. The Council consists of 21 

[6:1:66,269] voting members and 11 advisory members; the Chief Justice of California serves as chair. The Judicial Council staff serve as 

[6:2:66,279] the administrative arm of the Council. Staff provide policy support to the Council, administrative accountability in the operation 

[6:3:66,289] of the courts as specified by law, strategic planning for capital outlay, design, and construction of court facilities; and 

[6:4:66,299] administrative support for courts in areas such as budget, fiscal services, coordination of the assignment of retired judges, 

[6:5:66,310] technology, education, legal advice and services, human resources, legislative advocacy, and research. 

[6:6:66,327] 0140023 - JUDICIAL BRANCH FACILITY PROGRAM 

[7:0:66,345] The Judicial Branch Facility Program administers the acquisition, planning, construction, operations, and maintenance of 

[7:1:66,355] judicial branch facilities. This program is responsible for the development of long-term facilities master plans, facility and real 

[7:2:66,365] estate management, renovation of existing court facilities, and new courthouse planning, design, and construction. 

[7:3:66,383] 0150 - STATE TRIAL COURT FUNDING 

[7:4:66,401] 0150010 - SUPPORT FOR THE OPERATION OF THE TRIAL COURTS 

[8:0:66,419] This program's objective is to provide the resources necessary for the statewide trial court system to adjudicate civil and 

[8:1:66,429] criminal cases. This program includes all allowable trial court administrative costs under Government Code section 77003, 

[8:2:66,439] except court-appointed dependency counsel, salaries and benefits of Superior Court judges, compensation for assigned 

[8:3:66,449] judges, support for language interpreters, grants, and expenses on behalf of the trial courts. 

[8:4:66,467] 0150011 - COURT APPOINTED DEPENDENCY COUNSEL 

[9:0:66,485] This program provides funding for court-appointed counsel for children, non-minor dependents and parents in juvenile court 

[9:1:66,495] dependency proceedings pursuant to Welfare and Institutions Code section 317, subdivisions (b) and (c), and Government 

[9:2:66,505] Code section 77003, subdivision (a)(4). 

[9:3:66,523] 0150019 - COMPENSATION OF SUPERIOR COURT JUDGES 

[9:4:66,540] This program provides funding for the salaries and state benefits for Superior Court judges. 

[9:5:66,558] 0150028 - ASSIGNED JUDGES 

[10:0:66,576] This program provides support for the salaries and related costs of retired as well as active judges who are assigned by the 

[10:1:66,586] Chief Justice to positions in courts which require assistance due to judicial absences or other factors impacting the ability of a 

[10:2:66,596] court to avoid case delay. 

[10:3:66,614] 0150037 - COURT INTERPRETERS 

[11:0:66,632] This program supports the provision of qualified language interpreters in criminal, juvenile, family and civil proceedings as 

[11:1:66,642] required by statute. 

[11:2:66,659] 0150046 - GRANTS 

[12:0:66,677] This program provides funding for government grants providing public services related to the trial courts. 

[12:1:66,695] 0150095 - EXPENSES ON BEHALF OF THE TRIAL COURTS 

[13:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[2:0:207,38] 0250   Judicial Branch - Continued 

[3:0:66,65] This program provides appropriation for statewide programs and services provided to the court or courts by the Judicial Council 

[3:1:66,75] or payment for services or property of any kind contracted for the court or courts or on behalf of the courts by the Judicial 

[3:2:66,85] Council, which are paid for or reimbursed by the trial courts. The appropriation also supports the implementation and 

[3:3:66,95] administration of the Civil Representation Pilot Program. 

[3:4:66,113] 0155 - HABEAS CORPUS RESOURCE CENTER 

[4:0:66,131] The Habeas Corpus Resource Center provides legal representation for indigent petitioners in death penalty habeas corpus 

[4:1:66,141] proceedings before the Supreme Court of California and the federal courts. The Center also recruits and trains attorneys to 

[4:2:66,151] expand the pool of private counsel qualified to accept appointments in death penalty habeas corpus proceedings, serves as a 

[4:3:66,161] resource to them, and thereby helps to reduce the number of unrepresented indigents on California's death row.

[6:0:66,202] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW 

[7:0:66,220] The Judicial Branch consists of the Supreme Court, courts of appeal, trial courts, and the Judicial Council. The Supreme Court 

[7:1:66,230] is located in the Civic Center Plaza in San Francisco (101,500 square feet) and the Ronald Reagan State Building in Los 

[7:2:66,241] Angeles (7,600 sf). The courts of appeal are organized into six districts, operate in nine different locations, and consist of 

[7:3:66,251] approximately 508,000 sf. The trial courts are located in 58 counties statewide, in approximately 500 buildings and 2,100 

[7:4:66,261] courtrooms of approximately 14 million sf of usable area and more than 21 million sf of space under Judicial Council 

[7:5:66,271] responsibility and management. The facilities of the Supreme Court, courts of appeal, and trial courts encompass not only the 

[7:6:66,281] public courtroom spaces but also the chambers and workspaces where judicial officers and courtroom staff prepare for 

[7:7:66,292] proceedings; secure areas, including holding cells; and building support functions. Judicial Council administrative facilities, 

[7:8:66,302] occupying approximately 235,000 sf, are located in San Francisco and Sacramento. The Judicial Council consolidated field 

[7:9:66,312] offices to realize program efficiencies and cost savings, by closing its Burbank office on June 30, 2017, to consolidate 

[7:10:66,322] operations between Sacramento and San Francisco and by closing its Real Estate and Facilities Management field offices to 

[7:11:66,332] relocate staff to the court facilities to which they provide service. 

[8:0:66,369] MAJOR PROJECT CHANGES 

[8:1:65,388] •   Courthouse Construction-The Budget includes $1.3 billion lease revenue bond funds and $32.1 million special funds for the 

[8:2:76,398] continuing phases of ten active projects on the Judicial Council's immediate and critical needs list.

[0:0:66,13] 2018-19 STATE BUDGET — LJE   40   LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[1:0:180,43] 0280   Commission on Judicial Performance 

[2:0:66,64] The California Commission on Judicial Performance is the independent state agency responsible for investigating complaints of 

[2:1:66,74] judicial misconduct and judicial incapacity, and for disciplining judges pursuant to Article VI, Section 18 of the California 

[2:2:66,84] Constitution. It is the only body with such authority in the state. Its jurisdiction includes all active judges and justices of 

[2:3:66,95] California's superior courts, Courts of Appeal and Supreme Court, and former judges for conduct prior to retirement or 

[3:0:66,105] resignation. The Commission also shares authority with the local courts for the oversight of court commissioners and referees. 

[3:1:66,115] In addition to its disciplinary functions, the Commission is responsible for handling judges' applications for disability retirement. 

[4:0:66,135] The Commission's authority is limited to investigating alleged judicial misconduct and, if warranted, imposing discipline. Judicial 

[4:1:66,146] misconduct usually involves conduct in conflict with the standards set forth in the Code of Judicial Ethics. After investigation 

[4:2:66,156] and, in some cases a public hearing, the Commission may impose sanctions ranging from confidential discipline to removal 

[4:3:66,166] from office. 

[5:0:66,186] The Commission is composed of 11 members: 3 judges appointed by the Supreme Court; 2 attorneys appointed by the 

[6:0:66,197] Governor; and 6 lay citizens, of which 2 are appointed by the Governor, 2 are appointed by the Senate Committee on Rules, 

[6:1:66,207] and 2 are appointed by the Speaker of the Assembly. Members are appointed to four-year terms and may serve two terms. 

[6:2:66,217] Commission members do not receive a salary.

[0:0:66,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE   2018-19 STATE BUDGET — LJE   43 

[1:0:150,43] 0390   Contributions to the Judges' Retirement System 

[2:0:66,64] The Judges' Retirement System provides retirement benefit funding for judges on California's Supreme, Appellate, and 

[2:1:66,74] Superior/Municipal courts. The California Public Employees' Retirement System administers both the Judges' Retirement 

[2:2:66,84] System and the Judges' Retirement System II on behalf of its members. 

[3:0:66,105] The Judges' Retirement System II provides retirement, disability, and death benefits based on age, years of service, 

[3:1:66,115] compensation of active judges, and eligibility, as determined by specific sections of the Judges' Retirement Law. Active judges 

[3:2:66,125] and the state each contribute 8 percent of salary to the Judges' Retirement System. Additional contributions come from filing 

[3:3:66,135] fees for specific civil cases and investment income. These contributions, however, are not sufficient to fully fund benefit 

[3:4:66,146] payments. Consequently, current law requires the state to fund the difference between existing contribution sources and the 

[3:5:66,156] required benefit payments to retired judges. 

[4:0:66,176] The Judges' Retirement System is closed to new members, and a second retirement system for judges was established in 

[4:1:66,186] 1994. All new judges elected or appointed on or after November 9, 1994 become members of the Judges' Retirement System 

[4:2:66,197] II. The Judges' Retirement System II provides retirement, disability, and death benefits based on age, years of service, final 

[4:3:66,207] compensation, and eligibility, as determined by specific sections of the Judges' Retirement System II Law. Judges' Retirement 

[4:4:66,217] System II members eligible for a service retirement have the option of choosing the monetary credit plan (a lump-sum return of 

[4:5:66,227] contributions and interest earned) or the defined benefit plan. Judges' Retirement System II members not eligible for a defined 

[4:6:66,237] benefit receive the balance of their monetary credits (in a lump-sum payment). The Judges' Retirement System II receives 

[4:7:66,248] contributions from judges equal to 8 percent of salary as well as any investment income. The state's contributions are adjusted 

[4:8:66,258] annually to maintain actuarial soundness of the fund. 

[5:0:66,278] Effective January 1, 2013, provisions of the California Public Employees' Pension Reform Act of 2013, Chapter 296, Statutes of 

[5:1:66,288] 2012 (AB 340), require that new judges contribute 50 percent of the normal cost of their pension benefits.

[8:0:66,487] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES 

[8:1:65,505] •   Small Business Development Technical Assistance Expansion Program – The Budget includes $23 million General Fund to 

[8:2:76,515] establish a program to provide funding to augment existing small business services such as free or low-cost one-on-one 

[8:3:76,525] consulting and low-cost training. Of that amount, $17 million is provided each year, for the next five years to technical 

[9:0:76,535] assistance centers with a focus on services to underserved business groups, including   women, minority, and veteran-owned 

[9:1:76,546] businesses and businesses in low-wealth,   rural, and disaster-impacted communities. The Budget also includes the 

[10:0:76,556] continuation of $3 million in 2018-19 through 2022-23 for Small Business Development Centers' local cash match funding. 

[10:1:76,566] The remaining $3 million is one-time local cash match funding targeted for other federal small business technical assistance 

[10:2:76,576] centers. Additionally, the program will allocate funding through a competitive grant process. 

[11:0:65,592] •   Cannabis-Community Reinvestment Grants Program – The Budget includes $10 million California Cannabis Tax Fund and 

[11:1:76,603] three positions to administer the Program and to fund grants to local health departments and qualified community-based 

[11:2:76,613] nonprofit organizations that support various substance abuse treatment, job placement, legal services, and other cannabis 

[11:3:76,623] use-related programs in California, pursuant to Proposition 64.

[7:0:66,331] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[7:1:66,349] 0220 - GO-Biz 

[8:0:66,366] The GO-Biz serves as the Governor's lead entity for economic strategy and the marketing of California on issues relating to 

[8:1:66,377] business development, private sector investment, and economic growth, and export promotion. This program makes 

[8:2:66,387] recommendations to the Governor and the Legislature regarding policies, programs, and actions to advance statewide 

[8:3:66,397] economic goals. 

[9:0:66,415] 0225 - CALIFORNIA BUSINESS INVESTMENT SERVICES 

[10:0:66,432] This program serves employers, corporate executives, business owners, and site location consultants who are considering 

[10:1:66,443] California for business investment and expansion. This program convenes teams on key business development issues. This 

[10:2:66,453] program works with local, state, and federal partners to attract, retain, and grow businesses in addition to providing permit 

[10:3:66,463] assistance and helping businesses succeed in California. The Innovation Hub (iHub) initiative improves the state's national and 

[10:4:66,473] global competitiveness by stimulating partnerships, economic development, and job creation around specific research clusters 

[10:5:66,483] through state-designated iHubs. 

[11:0:66,501] 0230 - OFFICE OF THE SMALL BUSINESS ADVOCATE 

[12:0:66,519] The director of the Office of the Small Business Advocate (OSBA) serves as the principal advocate in the state on behalf of 

[12:1:66,529] small businesses, including legislation and administrative regulations that affect small business. The OSBA is responsible for 

[12:2:66,539] disseminating information about programs and services provided by the state that benefit small businesses, and how small 

[12:3:66,549] businesses can participate in these programs and services. The OSBA responds to issues from small businesses concerning 

[12:4:66,560] the actions of state agencies, state laws and regulations adversely affecting those businesses. The OSBA maintains and 

[12:5:66,570] distributes an annual list of persons serving as small business ombudsmen throughout state government. 

[12:6:66,588] 0235010 - CALIFORNIA FILM COMMISSION 

[13:0:66,605] The California Film Commission (CFC) enhances California's status as the leader in motion picture, television and commercial 

[13:1:66,615] production. The Commission is tasked with retaining and increasing motion picture production and to see that it continues to 

[13:2:66,626] create jobs and boost business throughout the State. A one-stop office for filmmakers, the Commission supports productions of 

[13:3:66,636] all sizes and budgets with a variety of services. In addition to issuing film permits for all state properties, administering the film 

[13:4:66,646] and TV tax credit program, maintaining an extensive location library, and offering production assistance on a wide variety of 

[13:5:66,656] issues, CFC also works closely with cities and counties with the goal of creating 'film friendly" policies that are consistent state-

[13:6:66,666] wide. 

[14:0:66,684] 0235019 - DIVISION OF TOURISM 

[15:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,65] The California Tourism Market Act provides for the marketing of California through an assessment of businesses that benefit 

[3:1:66,75] from travel and tourism. The objective of the Tourism Assessment Program is to identify potentially assessable businesses, 

[3:2:66,85] assist companies with determining the appropriate amount of their self-assessment, and collet the fee. 

[3:3:66,103] 0235028 - CALIFORINIA INFRASTRUCTURE AND ECONOMIC DEVELOPMENT BANK 

[4:0:66,121] California Infrastructure and Economic Development Bank (IBank) was created to finance public infrastructure and private 

[4:1:66,131] development that promote a healthy climate for jobs, contribute to a strong economy and improve the quality of life in California 

[4:2:66,141] communities. IBank has a broad authority to issue tax-exempt and taxable revenue bonds, provide financing to public 

[4:3:66,151] agencies, provide credit enhancements, acquire or lease facilities, and leverage State and Federal funds. IBank's current 

[4:4:66,161] programs include the infrastructure state revolving fund, 501(c)(3) tax-exempt and taxable revenue bond program, industrial 

[4:5:66,172] development revenue bond program, exempt facility revenue bond program, governmental bond program and the Clean 

[4:6:66,182] Energy Finance Center (CEFC) and the Statewide Energy Efficiency Program under the CEFC. 

[4:7:66,200] 0235037 - SMALL BUSINESS LOAN GUARANTEE PROGRAM 

[5:0:66,217] The Small Business Loan Guarantee Program (SBLGP) promotes local economic development by providing guarantees for 

[5:1:66,227] loans issued to small businesses from financial institutions, typically banks, that otherwise would not approve a term loan or 

[5:2:66,238] line of credit to a small business. As a result of the SBLGP, participating small businesses are able to secure financing that 

[5:3:66,248] allows them to grow and expand their business. The loan guarantee serves as a credit enhancement and an incentive for 

[5:4:66,258] financial institutions to make loans to small businesses that otherwise would not be eligible for such financing. 

[5:5:66,276] 0235046 - CALIFORNIA WELCOME CENTERS 

[6:0:66,293] California Welcome Centers are visitor information centers that are readily accessible to and recognizable by tourists to 

[6:1:66,304] encourage tourism in California and provide benefits to the state economy. The objective of the California Welcome Center 

[6:2:66,314] Program is to determine the locality of underserved travelers, designate a welcome center, and establish operating standards 

[6:3:66,324] across the network.

[1:0:117,43] 0530   Secretary for California Health and Human Services Agency 

[1:1:66,64] The primary mission of the Health and Human Services Agency (HHSA) is to provide policy leadership and direction to the 

[1:2:66,74] departments, board and programs it oversees, to reduce duplication and fragmentation among HHSA departments in policy 

[1:3:66,84] development and implementation, to improve coordination among departments on common programs, to ensure programmatic 

[1:4:66,95] integrity, and to advance the Governor's priorities on health and human services issues. 

[2:0:66,112] The HHSA accomplishes its mission through the administration and coordination of state and federal programs for public 

[2:1:66,123] health, health care services, social services, public assistance, health planning and licensing, and rehabilitation. These 

[2:2:66,133] programs touch the lives of millions of California's most needy and vulnerable residents. The HHSA is committed to striking a 

[2:3:66,143] balance between maintaining access to essential health and human services for California's most disadvantaged and at-risk 

[2:4:66,153] residents while pursuing ways to better manage and control costs. 

[2:5:66,171] The following departments and entities fall under the purview of the HHSA: 

[2:6:65,189] •   Department of Aging

[1:0:164,43] 0540   Secretary of the Natural Resources Agency 

[2:0:66,64] The mission of the Natural Resources Agency is to restore, protect and manage the state's natural, historical and cultural 

[3:0:66,74] resources for current and future generations using creative approaches and solutions based on science, collaboration and 

[3:1:66,84] respect for all involved communities. The secretary for Natural Resources, a member of the Governor's Cabinet, sets the 

[3:2:66,95] policies and coordinates the environmental preservation and restoration activities of 26 various departments, boards, 

[4:0:66,105] commissions and conservancies, and directly administers the Sea Grant Program, Ocean Protection Council, California 

[4:1:66,115] Environmental Quality Act, Environmental Enhancement Mitigation Program, River Parkways, Urban Greening, and the 

[4:2:66,125] California Cultural and Historical Endowment grant programs. 

[5:0:66,146] The Natural Resources Agency consists of the departments of Forestry and Fire Protection, Conservation, Fish and Wildlife, 

[5:1:66,156] Parks and Recreation, and Water Resources; the California Conservation Corps; Exposition Park; California Science Center; 

[5:2:66,166] California African American Museum; the State Lands Commission; the Colorado River Board; the San Francisco Bay 

[6:0:66,176] Conservation and Development Commission; the Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission; the Wildlife 

[6:1:66,186] Conservation Board; the Delta Protection Commission; the California Coastal Commission; the State Coastal Conservancy; the 

[6:2:66,197] California Tahoe Conservancy; the Santa Monica Mountains Conservancy; the Coachella Valley Mountains Conservancy; the 

[6:3:66,207] San Joaquin River Conservancy; the San Gabriel and Lower Los Angeles Rivers and Mountains Conservancy; the Baldwin 

[6:4:66,217] Hills Conservancy; the San Diego River Conservancy; the Sierra Nevada Conservancy; the Sacramento-San Joaquin Delta 

[6:5:66,227] Conservancy; the Native American Heritage Commission; and the Special Resources Program.

[1:0:199,43] 0552   Office of the Inspector General 

[2:0:66,64] The Office of the Inspector General (OIG) protects public safety by safeguarding the integrity of California's correctional 

[2:1:66,74] system. OIG is responsible for contemporaneous oversight of the California Department of Corrections and Rehabilitation's 

[2:2:66,84] (CDCR) internal affairs investigations, use of force, and the employee disciplinary process. When requested by the Governor, 

[2:3:66,95] the Senate Committee on Rules, or the Speaker of the Assembly, the Inspector General reviews the policies, practices, and 

[2:4:66,105] procedures of CDCR. The Inspector General reviews the Governor's candidates for appointment to serve as warden for the 

[2:5:66,115] state's adult correctional institutions and as superintendents for the state's juvenile facilities, and   conducts metric-oriented 

[2:6:66,125] inspection programs to periodically review delivery of medical care at each state prison. OIG receives communications from 

[2:7:66,135] individuals alleging improper governmental activity and maintains a toll-free public telephone number to receive allegations of 

[2:8:66,146] wrongdoing by employees of CDCR;   conducts formal reviews of complaints of retaliation from CDCR employees against upper 

[2:9:66,156] management where a legally cognizable cause of action is present; and reviews the mishandling of sexual abuse incidents 

[2:10:66,166] within correctional institutions. OIG provides critical public transparency for the state correctional system by publicly reporting 

[2:11:66,176] its findings. 

[3:0:66,197] In addition, OIG is responsible for the California Rehabilitation Oversight Board (Board) that examines CDCR's various mental 

[3:1:66,207] health, substance abuse, educational, and employment programs for inmates and parolees. The Board meets at least twice 

[3:2:66,217] annually to recommend modifications, additions, and eliminations of offender rehabilitation and treatment programs. The Board 

[3:3:66,227] also submits biannual reports to the Governor, the Legislature, and the public to convey its findings on the effectiveness of 

[3:4:66,237] treatment efforts, rehabilitation needs of offenders, gaps in offender rehabilitation services, and levels of offender participation 

[3:5:66,248] and success.

[1:0:175,43] 0555   Secretary for Environmental Protection 

[2:0:66,64] The Secretary for Environmental Protection is the cabinet level agency responsible for protecting human health and the 

[3:0:66,74] environment. California Environmental Protection Agency programs reduce greenhouse gas emissions, restore and protect 

[4:0:66,84] environmental quality, and protect public health. The Secretary coordinates the state's environmental regulatory programs and 

[4:1:66,95] ensures fair and consistent enforcement of environmental law, which safeguards the state's residents and promotes the state's 

[4:2:66,105] economic vitality. The Secretary also administers environmental justice, environmental law enforcement, emergency 

[4:3:66,115] preparedness and response, Certified Unified Program Agencies, and scientific review programs. The Secretary leads 

[5:0:66,125] greenhouse gas emission reduction and climate change activities in state government, and is responsible for coordinating the 

[5:1:66,135] implementation of Chapter 488, Statutes of 2006 (AB 32). The Secretary also centralizes coordination of California-Mexico 

[5:2:66,146] border environmental efforts.

[1:0:195,43] 0650   Office of Planning and Research 

[2:0:66,64] The Office of Planning and Research (OPR) assists the Governor and the Administration in planning, research, policy 

[2:1:66,74] development, and legislative analysis. OPR formulates long-range state goals and policies to address land use, climate 

[2:2:66,84] change, population growth and distribution, urban expansion, infrastructure development, groundwater sustainability and 

[2:3:66,95] drought response, and resource protection. OPR also maintains and updates the General Plan Guidelines, the California 

[2:4:66,105] Environmental Quality Act (CEQA) Guidelines, and operates the CEQA Clearinghouse, coordinating CEQA filings and state 

[2:5:66,115] compliance issues. OPR acts as the state's liaison to a variety of entities including local government, planning professionals, 

[2:6:66,125] small business, and the military. OPR houses and supports the Strategic Growth Council. In addition, OPR houses California 

[2:7:66,135] Volunteers, a program aimed to increase the number and impact of Californians involved with service and volunteering 

[2:8:66,146] throughout the state.

[5:0:66,373] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[6:0:66,392] 0360 - STATE PLANNING AND POLICY DEVELOPMENT 

[7:0:66,412] The major activities of the State Planning and Policy Development program include: (1) recommending and implementing state 

[7:1:66,422] policies on land-use and growth planning, including General Plan Guidelines; (2) coordinating the implementation of the 

[7:2:66,432] California Environmental Quality Act (CEQA), including: (a) operating the State Clearinghouse; (b) maintaining an online 

[7:3:66,443] database for the electronic submission and archiving of CEQA documents; (c) developing CEQA guidelines; (d) providing 

[7:4:66,453] technical assistance; (e) streamlining permitting; and (f) providing training; (3) supporting the Governor's Advisor for Military 

[7:5:66,463] Affairs and Governor's Military Council; (4) providing policy research for the Governor and Cabinet; (5) providing technical 

[7:6:66,473] advice to, and developing guidelines for, local governments on land use planning and compliance with environmental statutes 

[7:7:66,483] and regulations; (6) developing a program to coordinate regional and local efforts with state climate adaptation strategies 

[7:8:66,494] regarding the impacts of climate change; (7) supporting drought and fire response efforts; (8) supporting development of state 

[7:9:66,504] plans including, but not limited to, the Water Action Plan, the State Multi-Hazard Mitigation Plan, the Safeguarding California 

[7:10:66,514] Plan, the Integrated Energy Policy Report, and the 5-Year Infrastructure Plan; (9) supporting multiple efforts to address climate 

[7:11:66,524] change and response to carbon pollution; (10) administering the Integrated Climate Adaptation and Resiliency Program to 

[7:12:66,534] coordinate regional and local efforts with state climate adaptation strategies to adapt to the impacts of climate change; (11) 

[7:13:66,545] serving as the primary point of contact for information on federal grants related to community, economic, and local 

[7:14:66,555] development, as well as the coordinator to manage and maximize federal grant opportunities within the state; (12) 

[8:0:66,565] administering CENSUS 2020 funding, Land Conservation and Conveyance Plan funding, and Precision Medicine funding; (13) 

[8:1:66,575] conducting a study reviewing the Master Plan for Higher Education; (14) establishing the Rural Economic Development 

[8:2:66,585] Steering Committee to prioritize sustainable rural economic development in alignment with the state's existing climate goals; 

[8:3:66,596] and (15) conducting other activities as the Governor may direct. 

[8:4:66,613] 0365 - CALIFORNIA VOLUNTEERS 

[9:0:66,634] California Volunteers is charged with increasing the number and impact of Californians involved with service and volunteering 

[9:1:66,644] throughout the state. California Volunteers administers programs such as AmeriCorps and Disaster Volunteering and 

[9:2:66,654] Preparedness, guides policy development to support the nonprofit and service fields, and leads the Service Enterprise 

[10:0:66,664] Initiative, which empowers a nonprofit to more efficiently engage volunteers and effectively address community needs. Through 

[10:1:66,674] the efforts of California Volunteers, Californians of all ages and abilities are provided ongoing opportunities to volunteer, to 

[10:2:66,685] become better prepared to respond in emergencies and disasters, and ultimately, to embrace a lifetime ethic of service.

[2:0:160,38] 0650   Office of Planning and Research - Continued 

[3:0:66,65] The Council coordinates interagency efforts and partners with local and regional government stakeholders to promote 

[3:1:66,75] sustainable development, improving air and water quality, protecting natural resources and agricultural lands, and reducing 

[3:2:66,85] greenhouse gas emissions. The Council administers the Transformative Climate Communities Program, the Affordable 

[3:3:66,95] Housing and Sustainable Communities Program, and the Sustainable Agricultural Lands Conservation Program, developing 

[3:4:66,106] guidelines, reviewing applications, and providing funding as part of greenhouse gas reduction efforts associated with cap and 

[3:5:66,116] trade funds. The Council also administers a technical assistance program to support all Greenhouse Gas Reduction Fund 

[3:6:66,126] programs to assist in the development of projects that maximize greenhouse gas reductions. The Council administers Climate 

[3:7:66,136] Change and Adaptation Research Program funding. The Council is charged with review of the California 5-Year Infrastructure 

[3:8:66,146] plan and with making grants and loans to institutions for planning and implementing land uses that achieve the goals of the 

[3:9:66,157] State's Planning Priorities. The Council also oversees the Health in All Policies program, and sponsors research on infill 

[3:10:66,167] development, conservation, and other planning issues.

[0:0:66,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE   2018-19 STATE BUDGET — LJE   109 

[1:0:202,43] 0690   Office of Emergency Services 

[2:0:66,64] The principal objective of the Office of Emergency Services (OES) is to protect lives and property, build capabilities, and 

[3:0:66,74] support our communities for a resilient California. The OES Director serves as both the Governor's Homeland Security Advisor 

[3:1:66,84] and Emergency Management Director, with responsibility to implement and facilitate the state's homeland security and counter-

[3:2:66,95] terrorism strategy. The OES serves the public through effective collaboration in preparing for, protecting against, responding to, 

[3:3:66,105] recovering from, and mitigating the impacts of all hazards and threats. 

[4:0:66,123] On a day-to-day basis, the OES provides leadership, assistance, training, and support to state and local agencies and 

[5:0:66,133] coordinates with federal agencies in planning and preparing for the most effective use of federal, state, local, and private sector 

[5:1:66,143] resources in emergencies. This emergency planning is based upon a system of mutual aid whereby a jurisdiction relies first on 

[5:2:66,153] its own resources, and then requests assistance from its neighbors. The OES' plans and programs are coordinated with those 

[5:3:66,163] of the federal government, other states, private sector, utilities, and state and local agencies within California. 

[6:0:66,181] During an emergency, the OES functions as the Governor's immediate staff to provide guidance and coordinate the state's 

[6:1:66,191] responsibilities under the Emergency Services Act and applicable federal statutes. It also acts as the conduit for federal 

[6:2:66,201] assistance through natural disaster grants and federal agency support. Additionally, the OES is responsible for the 

[7:0:66,212] development and coordination of a comprehensive state strategy related to all hazards, including terrorism, which includes 

[7:1:66,222] prevention, preparedness, mitigation, response, and recovery. 

[8:0:66,240] The OES implements the state homeland security strategy by overseeing the State Threat Assessment System (STAS), which 

[8:1:66,250] includes management of the State Threat Assessment Center (STAC). The STAS is comprised of four regional and one major 

[8:2:66,260] urban area Fusion Centers that assist in the detection, prevention, investigation, and response to criminal and terrorist activity; 

[8:3:66,270] and information and intelligence sharing between local, state, federal, tribal agencies, and private sector partners. The STAC 

[9:0:66,280] operates as the state's information-sharing clearinghouse for strategic threat analysis, counter-terrorism, situational awareness 

[9:1:66,291] reporting, and case analysis for statewide leadership, agencies, and public safety community. Further, the OES improves the 

[9:2:66,301] criminal justice system in California by providing financial and technical assistance to local governments, state agencies, and 

[9:3:66,311] the private sector for homeland security, public safety, and victim services. 

[10:0:66,329] Because the OES' programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the OES has a capital outlay program to support this 

[10:1:66,339] need. For the specifics on the OES' capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[6:0:66,272] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES 

[6:1:65,290] •   California Disaster Assistance Act – The Budget includes a one-time augmentation of $88.1 million General Fund to increase 

[6:2:76,300] the amount of funding available through the California Disaster Assistance Act, which is used to repair,   restore, or replace 

[6:3:76,310] public real property damaged or destroyed by a disaster, and to reimburse local governments for costs associated with 

[6:4:76,321] certain emergency activities undertaken in response to a state of emergency.   This augmentation increases the total funding 

[6:5:76,331] available to $127.2 million in 2018-19. 

[7:0:65,347] •   California Public Safety Microwave Network – The Budget includes $15 million State Emergency Telephone Number Account 

[7:1:76,357] to begin a five-year plan to upgrade the California Public Safety Microwave Network from an analog system to a digital 

[7:2:76,367] system to enhance emergency response communications. 

[8:0:65,384] •   Next Generation 9-1-1 – The Budget includes $11.5 million State Emergency Telephone Number Account to implement a 

[8:1:76,394] statewide Next Generation 9-1-1 system, which will provide various benefits, including faster call delivery, increased routing 

[8:2:76,404] accuracy and functionality, call overflow and backup functionality,   updated geographic information capability and wireless 

[8:3:76,414] location data, and incoming text capability. 

[9:0:65,430] •   California Earthquake Early Warning System – The Budget includes $15.8 million General Fund to complete the buildout of 

[9:1:76,441] the California Earthquake Early Warning System and provide support to the California Earthquake Early Warning Program 

[9:2:76,451] and Advisory Board. 

[10:0:65,467] •   Situational Awareness and Collaboration Tool – The Budget includes $353,000 General Fund and $325,000   reimbursements 

[10:1:76,477] to manage and train local agencies on the effective use of this tactical tool, which is deployed in the field to coordinate 

[10:2:76,487] various disaster response efforts, including, but not limited to, evacuations, warnings, and shelters. 

[10:3:65,504] •   Emergency Response Operations – The Budget includes $1.6 million General Fund to increase staffing within the Homeland 

[10:4:76,514] Security Branch and Regional Response and Readiness Branch.   These branches support liaison efforts between federal, 

[10:5:76,524] state, and local partners, and assist with responding to disasters and emergency management coordination. 

[10:6:65,540] •   Recovery Program Workload – The Budget includes $2.8 million General Fund and $8.4 million Federal Trust Fund for three 

[10:7:76,550] years to address increased workload related to disaster recovery activities, including working with impacted communities to 

[10:8:76,561] implement and administer California Disaster Assistance Act and federal disaster programs. 

[10:9:65,577] •   Hazard Mitigation Program – The Budget includes $3.2 million Federal Trust Fund   for three years to enhance hazard 

[10:10:76,587] mitigation activities throughout the state.   Mitigation programs provide funding for eligible activities that reduce disaster 

[10:11:76,597] losses and protect life and property from future disaster damages. 

[11:0:65,613] •   Regional Hazardous Materials Response Program – The Budget includes $3.4 million General Fund for three years to 

[11:1:76,624] provide regional and on-site support for planning, response, and mitigation capabilities in the event of a release of hazardous 

[11:2:76,634] materials. 

[12:0:65,650] •   Domestic Violence Housing Program – The Budget includes $10 million General Fund one-time to support domestic violence 

[12:1:76,660] services providers for projects that include emergency safe homes or shelters for victims and their families. 

[12:2:65,676] •   Domestic Violence and Sexual Assault Prevention Services – The Budget includes $10 million General Fund one-time to 

[12:3:76,687] support services for individuals impacted by domestic violence and sexual assault. 

[13:0:65,703] •   Family Justice Centers – The Budget includes $10 million General Fund one-time to provide grants to Family Justice Centers 

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[1:0:410,24] 2018-19 STATE BUDGET — LJE   115 

[2:0:167,38] 0690   Office of Emergency Services - Continued 

[3:0:66,72] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[4:0:66,91] 0380 - EMERGENCY MANAGEMENT SERVICES 

[5:0:66,108] Through the Emergency Management Services and Homeland Security Program, the OES provides effective use of federal, 

[5:1:66,119] state, and local resources, through the mutual aid system to jurisdictions whose resources and services are overextended in a 

[5:2:66,129] disaster situation. Also, this program develops and coordinates a comprehensive state strategy related to an all-hazards 

[5:3:66,139] approach that includes prevention, preparedness, mitigation, response, and recovery consistent with the National Strategy on 

[5:4:66,149] Homeland Security. Central to this effort is maintaining operational readiness at all levels of government. Within this program, 

[5:5:66,159] the OES also trains emergency managers, responders, and state and local leaders in preparedness and response to all 

[5:6:66,170] hazards. 

[6:0:66,187] 0385 - SPECIAL PROGRAMS AND GRANT MANAGEMENT 

[7:0:66,205] The OES serves as the State Administering Agent for federal homeland security, emergency management, and victim services 

[7:1:66,215] grants and is the primary liaison with the Federal Emergency Management Agency, the United States Department of Homeland 

[7:2:66,225] Security, and other federal agencies. Additionally, the OES serves as an advocate for local public safety, emergency 

[7:3:66,236] management, and public and private agencies operating public safety and victim service programs. As part of its grant 

[7:4:66,246] management role, the OES administers the California Disaster Assistance Act and provides eligible local agencies with 

[7:5:66,256] financial assistance to recover from disasters. Included in this program is the State Threat Assessment Center which is the 

[7:6:66,266] centerpiece of the State's intelligence information sharing environment and serves as the Governor's designated primary fusion 

[7:7:66,276] center. 

[8:0:66,294] 0395 - PUBLIC SAFETY COMMUNICATIONS 

[9:0:66,312] Public Safety Communications (PSC) serves the State of California by providing public safety communications to the State's 

[9:1:66,322] first responders and oversight of the 9-1-1 system to the people of California. The PSC is dedicated to the preservation and 

[9:2:66,332] protection of human life and public safety by delivering reliable and dependable communication services keeping the public 

[9:3:66,342] connected during times of crisis. 

[9:4:66,360] 9900 - ADMINISTRATION AND EXECUTIVE 

[10:0:66,378] The Administration and Executive Program provides the overall policy direction of the OES and support services such as 

[10:1:66,388] accounting, fiscal, personnel, information technology, and business services.

[5:0:66,278] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW 

[6:0:66,296] The Office of Emergency Services' infrastructure includes a headquarters facility, an administrative building, and an Inland 

[6:1:66,306] Regional Operations Center in Sacramento County; a Coastal Regional Operations Center in Walnut Creek, Contra Costa 

[6:2:66,316] County; a Southern Regional Operations Center located at Los Alamitos Joint Forces Training Base in Orange County; the 

[6:3:66,326] California Specialized Training Institute at Camp San Luis Obispo; and various small field offices throughout the state.

[6:5:66,354] throughout the state. These locations include 8 area offices and 37 area shops, positioned geographically to facilitate 

[6:6:66,365] maintenance and installation services to remote communication sites and customers throughout the state. In addition, the PSC 

[6:7:66,375] maintains and operates 10 communications vaults/towers and a total of more than 3,500 radio frequency points of presence.

[1:0:175,43] 0730   Governor Elect and Outgoing Governor 

[1:1:66,64] These funds are to provide assistance to the Governor-elect and the outgoing Governor, during the transition period following 

[1:2:66,74] the election, in carrying out the duties described under the provisions of Government Code Sections 12015 and 12015.5. 

[2:0:66,95] The law provides that state agencies furnish to the Governor-elect any information or assistance necessary in the preparation 

[2:1:66,105] of the annual state budget and for the orderly transfer of the executive power. This gives the Governor-elect approximately two 

[2:2:66,115] months to formulate policies and to incorporate them in the various programs included in the Budget, which will be transmitted 

[2:3:66,125] to the Legislature. 

[3:0:66,146] The law also provides for the outgoing Governor to appoint persons for up to a two-month period, to assist in concluding 

[3:1:66,156] matters arising from official duties during the last term of office. 

[4:0:66,176] Of the $1,000,000 budgeted, $865,000 is for the Governor-elect and $135,000 is for the outgoing Governor.

[1:0:191,43] 0750   Office of the Lieutenant Governor 

[2:0:66,64] Under California's Constitution, the Lieutenant Governor serves as Acting Governor whenever the Governor is absent from the 

[2:1:66,74] state, and automatically becomes Governor if a vacancy occurs in the Office of Governor. The Lieutenant Governor is also 

[2:2:66,84] President of the Senate and votes in case of a tie. 

[3:0:66,105] The Lieutenant Governor serves as a voting member of the Board of Regents of the University of California and a voting 

[3:1:66,115] member of the Board of Trustees of the California State University system. 

[4:0:66,135] The Lieutenant Governor also serves on, and rotates with the State Controller, as chair of the three-member State Lands 

[4:1:66,146] Commission, which oversees the control and leasing of millions of acres of state-owned land, including offshore oil resources, 

[4:2:66,156] as well as use and permitting for all navigable waterways in California. The Commission also manages state land-use planning 

[4:3:66,166] and revenues, and related interstate issues. During alternate years, when the Lieutenant Governor serves as Chairperson of 

[4:4:66,176] the State Lands Commission, he also serves as a member of the Ocean Protection Council. 

[5:0:66,197] In addition, under state statutes, the Lieutenant Governor chairs the California Commission for Economic Development, which 

[5:1:66,207] provides support and guidance to the Governor, Legislature and private sector regarding the development of California's 

[5:2:66,217] economy.

[1:0:224,43] 0820   Department of Justice 

[2:0:66,64] The constitutional office of the Attorney General, as chief law officer of the state, has the responsibility to see that the laws of 

[3:0:66,74] California are uniformly and adequately enforced. This responsibility is fulfilled through the diverse programs of the Department 

[3:1:66,84] of Justice. 

[4:0:66,105] The Department of Justice is responsible for providing skillful and efficient legal services on behalf of the people of California. 

[4:1:66,115] The Attorney General represents the people in all matters before the Appellate and Supreme Courts of California and the 

[5:0:66,125] United States; serves as legal counsel to state officers, boards, commissioners, and departments; represents the people in 

[5:1:66,135] actions to protect the environment and to enforce consumer, antitrust, and civil laws; and assists district attorneys in the 

[6:0:66,146] administration of justice. The Department provides oversight, enforcement, education, and regulation of California's firearms/ 

[6:1:66,156] dangerous weapon laws; provides evaluation and analysis of physical evidence; regulates legal gambling activities in 

[7:0:66,166] California; supports the telecommunications and data processing needs of the California criminal justice community; and 

[7:1:66,176] pursues projects designed to protect the people of California from fraudulent, unfair, and illegal activities.

[8:0:66,497] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES 

[8:1:65,515] •   Sex Offenders: Registration (Senate Bill 384) – The Budget includes $10 million General Fund and 25 positions to begin 

[8:2:76,525] implementing Chapter 541, Statutes of 2017 (SB 384), which will replace the existing lifetime sex offender registration 

[8:3:76,536] systems with a tiered registration system. 

[9:0:65,552] •   Audit of Sexual Assault Kit Backlog   - The Budget   includes $1 million General Fund for the compilation of information on the 

[9:1:76,562] number of untested sexual assault kits statewide. The resources provided are available for grants to counties and cities to 

[9:2:76,572] count the number of untested sexual assault kits in their possession. 

[9:3:65,588] •   Sexual Assault Kit Backlog Testing - The Budget   includes $6.5 million General Fund   for the processing of sexual assault 

[9:4:76,599] evidence. The resources provided are available for grants to counties and cities. In awarding grants, the Department of 

[9:5:76,609] Justice shall prioritize applications to eliminate existing backlogs of unprocessed evidence and all grantees shall provide a 

[9:6:76,619] dollar-for-dollar match to any state grant funds awarded. 

[10:0:65,635] •   DNA Identification Fund Backfill – The Budget   includes $6 million General Fund to backfill the decline in fine and fee 

[10:1:76,645] revenues in the DNA Identification Fund.   This funding will enable the Department of Justice to continue processing forensic 

[10:2:76,656] evidence for client counties at existing levels. 

[11:0:65,672] •   Bureau of Forensic Services Equipment Replacement – The Budget   includes $5.4 million General Fund to replace critical 

[11:1:76,682] forensic equipment within the Bureau of Forensic Services. 

[12:0:65,698] •   Immigration Data Governance (SB 54) Implementation – The Budget includes $2.4 million General Fund and 13 positions to 

[12:1:76,708] implement the provisions of SB 54, including promulgating model policies, collecting data from local law enforcement

[4:0:66,100] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[5:0:66,119] 0435 - LEGAL SERVICES 

[6:0:66,136] Legal Services is organized into three elements: (1) Civil Law, (2) Criminal Law, and (3) Public Rights. 

[7:0:66,154] Civil Law represents the State of California and its officers, agencies, departments, boards, commissions, and employees in 

[7:1:66,164] civil matters. It provides advice to these clients, defends cases brought against them and prosecutes cases to vindicate state 

[7:2:66,174] interests. Deputy Attorneys General in Civil Law are responsible for managing and litigating cases before administrative 

[7:3:66,185] tribunals, and in both state and federal courts at the trial level and on appeal, including appeals before the United States and 

[7:4:66,195] California Supreme Courts. Deputies work in one of eight sections: Business and Tax; Correctional Law; Employment and 

[7:5:66,205] Administrative Mandates; Government Law; Health, Education and Welfare; Health Quality Enforcement; Licensing; or Tort and 

[7:6:66,215] Condemnation. 

[8:0:66,233] Criminal Law represents the state in criminal matters before the Appellate and Supreme courts. Criminal Law also fulfills the 

[8:1:66,243] Attorney General's responsibilities of assisting district attorneys in cases for which they are recused, conducts criminal 

[8:2:66,253] investigations, represents the Governor, Board of Parole Hearings, and California Department of Corrections and Rehabilitation 

[8:3:66,264] in state and federal habeas corpus cases and appeals, and other proceedings relating to parole decisions and conditions of 

[8:4:66,274] confinement in the state prisons and defends state and federal habeas corpus matters. Additional responsibilities include 

[8:5:66,284] enforcing the Political Reform Act, advising the Governor on extradition matters, investigating and prosecuting Medi-Cal 

[8:6:66,294] provider fraud, investigating and prosecuting the abuse or neglect of elder and dependent adults residing in long-term health 

[8:7:66,304] care facilities, and investigating, prosecuting, and coordinating litigation involving white-collar crime, high-tech/computer/privacy 

[8:8:66,315] crime, financial crimes against the elderly, human trafficking, environmental crimes, and public corruption. 

[9:0:66,332] Public Rights protects and preserves the public interest by providing legal services to state agencies and Constitutional Officers 

[9:1:66,342] and by bringing actions in the name of the Attorney General to protect the public. Public Rights provides specialized services in 

[9:2:66,353] the following areas: Civil Rights Enforcement (including Underground Economy and the Bureau of Children's Justice); 

[9:3:66,363] Charitable Trusts (including the Registry of Charitable Trusts); Natural Resources Law; Corporate Fraud (including False 

[9:4:66,373] Claims, Energy and Corporate Responsibility); Indian and Gaming Law; Environment Law; Land Law; Consumer Law 

[9:5:66,383] (including Sellers of Travel and Privacy Enforcement and Protection); Antitrust Law; and Tobacco Litigation Enforcement. 

[10:0:66,401] 0440 - LAW ENFORCEMENT 

[11:0:66,419] The Division of Law Enforcement is organized into five elements: (1) Bureau of Firearms, (2) Bureau of Forensic Services, (3) 

[11:1:66,429] Bureau of Gambling Control, (4) Bureau of Investigation, and (5) the Office of the Chief. 

[12:0:66,447] The Bureau of Firearms provides oversight, enforcement, education, and regulation of California's firearms/dangerous weapon 

[12:1:66,457] laws by conducting firearms eligibility background checks and administering over thirty different state-mandated firearms-

[12:2:66,467] related programs. The Bureau conducts firearms dealer and manufacturer inspections and provides training as needed. Special 

[12:3:66,477] Agents conduct investigations on armed and prohibited persons and other investigations resulting in the seizure of weapons. 

[12:4:66,487] Agents also conduct firearms investigations to prevent illegal gun trafficking at in-state and out-of-state gun shows in 

[12:5:66,498] accordance with state and federal law. 

[13:0:66,515] The Bureau of Forensic Services (BFS) provides evaluation and analysis of physical evidence, including crime scene 

[13:1:66,525] investigation and expert court testimony to federal, state and local law enforcement agencies, district attorneys, and courts, by 

[13:2:66,536] operating 11 specialized laboratories that serve 46 counties as well as a forensic training facility. BFS maintains the state DNA 

[13:3:66,546] laboratory database which compiles DNA profiles of sex and violent offenders and felony arrestees. 

[14:0:66,564] The Bureau of Gambling Control regulates legal gambling activities in California to ensure gambling is conducted honestly and 

[14:1:66,574] is free from criminal and corruptive elements. This is accomplished by investigating the qualifications of individuals and 

[14:2:66,584] business entities who apply for state gambling licenses and monitoring the conduct of these licensees to ensure compliance 

[14:3:66,594] with the Gambling Control Act and applicable regulations. Furthermore, the Bureau conducts criminal investigations in, on or 

[14:4:66,604] about Tribal casinos and California cardrooms. The Bureau also regulates Tribal gaming to ensure that each Tribe is in 

[14:5:66,615] compliance with all aspects of the negotiated gaming compact. 

[15:0:66,632] The Bureau of Investigation is the premiere investigative agency that is responsible for exploiting and dismantling criminal 

[15:1:66,642] organizations, as well as assisting with the prosecution of serious criminal offenses which present a significant and multi-

[15:2:66,653] jurisdictional threat to California. The Bureau prioritizes investigations related to transnational criminal organizations involved in 

[15:3:66,663] gangs and human trafficking, as well as cases involving environmental crimes, public corruption, major fraud, underground 

[15:4:66,673] economy, and high-technology crimes. In addition, the Bureau serves a principal role in providing leadership, coordination, and 

[15:5:66,683] support to law enforcement through multi-agency drug, gang and major crimes task forces statewide. 

[16:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[1:0:382,24] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[2:0:189,38] 0820   Department of Justice - Continued 

[3:0:66,65] The Office of the Chief enhances public safety by providing training, technical, and administrative support to the investigative, 

[3:1:66,75] regulatory and forensic components of the Division of Law Enforcement and other criminal justice agencies. The Office serves 

[3:2:66,85] as the policy-making and oversight body for its four operational bureaus. 

[3:3:66,103] 0445 - CALIFORNIA JUSTICE INFORMATION SERVICES 

[4:0:66,121] The California Justice Information Services Division provides criminal justice intelligence, information, and identification 

[4:1:66,131] services to law enforcement, regulatory agencies, and the public. Four major functional areas carry out these primary services: 

[4:2:66,141] 1) The Bureau of Criminal Identification & Investigative Services consolidates the identification, investigative, and field services 

[4:3:66,151] functions, providing information and technical assistance on manual and automated systems including the fingerprint 

[4:4:66,161] identification system and the violent crime information system; 2) The Bureau of Criminal Information and Analysis consolidates 

[4:5:66,172] the functions related to the authorization, release, and use of criminal offender record information for law enforcement 

[4:6:66,182] investigatory and regulatory purposes; 3) The Hawkins Data Center operates the Criminal Justice Information System and the 

[4:7:66,192] California Law Enforcement Telecommunications System; and 4) the Operations Support Program provides business 

[4:8:66,202] resumption planning and administrative support and oversight. 

[4:9:66,220] 9900 - DIRECTORATE AND ADMINISTRATION 

[5:0:66,238] The Directorate and the Administration Division of the Department of Justice consists of the Division of Operations and the 

[5:1:66,248] Attorney General's Executive Office. The executive office maintains overall direction and administration over the diverse 

[5:2:66,258] programs and projects of the department, including the Equal Employment Rights and Resolution Office, the Office of Program 

[5:3:66,268] Oversight and Accountability, the Opinions Unit, the Solicitor General's Unit, the Office of Legislative Affairs, the Office of 

[5:4:66,278] Communications and the Public Inquiry Unit. In addition, the Division of Operations provides support functions essential to the 

[5:5:66,289] department's operations, including fiscal, personnel, and specialized services such as legal secretarial support, litigation 

[5:6:66,299] support, and legal case management services.

[0:0:66,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE   2018-19 STATE BUDGET — LJE   163 

[1:0:242,43] 0840   State Controller 

[2:0:66,64] The State Controller is the Chief Fiscal Officer of California, the fifth largest economy in the world. Principally responsible for 

[2:1:66,74] transparency and accountability of the state's financial resources, the Controller ensures the appropriate disbursement and 

[2:2:66,84] tracking of taxpayer dollars. The Controller serves on dozens of state boards, commissions, and committees with duties 

[2:3:66,95] ranging from administrative oversight of the nation's two largest public pension funds, to protection of state lands and 

[2:4:66,105] coastlines, and modernization and financing of major infrastructure. The Controller also offers fiscal guidance to local 

[3:0:66,115] governments and has independent auditing authority over government agencies related to the spending of state funds. The 

[3:1:66,125] Controller's primary objectives are to: 

[4:0:65,143] •   Account for and control disbursement of all state funds, issuing warrants in payment of the state's bills, including lottery 

[4:1:76,153] prizes. 

[5:0:65,169] •   Determine legality and accuracy of financial claims against the state. 

[5:1:65,186] •   Audit state and local government programs. 

[6:0:65,202] •   Safeguard many types of assets until claimed by the rightful owners, in accordance with the Unclaimed Property Law. 

[6:1:65,218] •   Inform the public of the state's financial condition. 

[7:0:65,234] •   Inform the public of financial transactions of city, county, and other local governments. 

[7:1:65,250] •   Administer the Uniform State Payroll System. 

[8:0:65,267] •   Audit and process all personnel and payroll transactions for state civil service, state exempt employees, state university 

[8:1:76,277] employees, and college system employees. 

[9:0:66,307] 3-YEAR EXPENDITURES AND POSITIONS
