[3:0:66,65] state; and collaborates with the Department of Finance, the Treasurer, and the Department of General Services to develop and 

[3:1:66,75] implement the Financial Information System for California (FI$Cal) to ensure the system meets the state's primary objectives 

[3:2:66,85] and provides best business practices for the state. 

[4:0:66,103] The Local Government Programs and Services Division maintains a database of information and reports on local financial 

[4:1:66,113] transactions and government compensation; apportions shared revenues to local governments; administers local mandated 

[4:2:66,123] cost programs; approves county cost allocation plans; prescribes uniform accounting procedures for presentation of financial 

[4:3:66,134] data for local governments and trial court revenues; administers and collects estate and inheritance taxes; collects delinquent 

[4:4:66,144] insurance and motor vehicle fuel taxes and petroleum and gas assessments; refunds gasoline taxes paid on fuel consumed for 

[4:5:66,154] off-highway purposes; instructs and advises county tax collectors; and administers the Property Tax Postponement Program for 

[4:6:66,164] senior and disabled citizens. 

[5:0:66,182] 0500200 - AUDITS 

[6:0:66,200] The Audits Division determines the legality and accuracy of all claims against the state through the performance of prepayment 

[6:1:66,210] audits; assures the accuracy of local government claims and financial statements submitted to the state and federal 

[6:2:66,220] governments by establishing and updating audit guidelines, and reviewing audits performed by independent auditors; performs 

[6:3:66,230] field audits for a variety of state and federal programs; reviews the single audits reports of local governments and local 

[6:4:66,240] education agencies that receive "pass-through federal funds" from state agencies; and audits major businesses for compliance 

[6:5:66,251] with the Unclaimed Property Law. 

[7:0:66,268] 0500300 - PERSONNEL/PAYROLL SERVICES 

[8:0:66,286] The Personnel/Payroll Services Division administers the state's payroll, employment history, the automated travel expense 

[8:1:66,296] reimbursement system, and leave accounting processes in a manner that provides accurate, timely and efficient processing for 

[8:2:66,306] the users of these systems. The Division works with other entities, e.g., Department of Human Resources, Judicial Council, 

[8:3:66,317] Department of Finance, and California State University Chancellor's Office, to maximize the efficiency of the state's personnel 

[8:4:66,327] and payroll functions, while continuing to maintain the highest level and quality of customer service. 

[9:0:66,344] The California State Payroll System Project was initiated to modernize and replace existing statewide human resource 

[9:1:66,355] management systems as part of a fully integrated solution. The new system should enable the state to improve management 

[9:2:66,365] processes and fulfill its payroll, reporting, and deduction processing obligations accurately and on time. 

[10:0:66,383] 0500400 - UNCLAIMED PROPERTY 

[11:0:66,400] The Unclaimed Property Division administers the Unclaimed Property Law by providing one central source for owners to claim 

[11:1:66,410] their property; notifying owners of their unclaimed property before it is transferred to the state; receiving unclaimed property 

[11:2:66,421] from banks, savings and loans, and other business firms; and providing outreach and education for California businesses 

[11:3:66,431] holding unclaimed property. 

[12:0:66,449] 0500500 - DISBURSEMENTS 

[13:0:66,466] The Disbursements Bureau provides services to all state Agencies, Departments and Boards. The primary responsibility is to 

[13:1:66,476] produce and deliver all payments of the state's obligations after auditing and clearance through control accounts, including 

[13:2:66,487] personal income tax refunds, payroll, and retirement payments. Disbursements also provides post issuance services, including, 

[13:3:66,497] but not limited to disposition of undeliverable warrants; lost warrant duplication; and maintenance and production of paid 

[13:4:66,507] warrant images. 

[14:0:66,525] 9900100 - ADMINISTRATION 

[15:0:66,542] The Executive Office and the Administration Division provide executive direction and support services to programs in the State 

[15:1:66,553] Controller's Office. The Information Systems Division develops, maintains, and operates all of the department's mainframe, 

[15:2:66,563] client-server and web-based systems; and oversees the planning, procurement, use, and maintenance of information 

[15:3:66,573] technology hardware and software.

[1:0:216,43] 0845   Department of Insurance 

[2:0:66,64] California's publicly-elected Insurance Commissioner regulates the sixth largest insurance economy in the world with insurers 

[2:1:66,74] collecting more than $289 billion in premium annually in California while protecting consumers and the integrity, health and 

[2:2:66,84] vitality of the insurance marketplace. The vision of the California Department of Insurance (CDI) is "Insurance Protection for All 

[2:3:66,95] Californians." CDI accomplishes this by enforcing insurance laws and regulations, assisting consumers in their dealings with 

[2:4:66,105] insurers, and using innovation to improve services for insurance producers and consumers. 

[3:0:66,123] The CDI licenses and regulates insurance companies, and individuals in California. Currently, the CDI oversees and licenses 

[3:1:66,133] approximately 1,300 insurance companies and more than 400,000 individuals and business entities as insurance agents, 

[3:2:66,143] brokers, adjusters and bail agents. Additionally, the CDI receives and investigates approximately 200,000 consumer inquiries 

[3:3:66,153] and complaints annually; performs examinations to ensure the financial solvency of companies; receives more than 28,000 

[3:4:66,163] suspected fraudulent claim referrals annually; receives and reviews approximately 7,250 rate filing applications annually; and 

[3:5:66,174] works in conjunction with local, state and federal law enforcement agencies to investigate and prosecute fraudulent insurance 

[3:6:66,184] practices.

[7:0:66,446] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[8:0:66,464] 0520 - REGULATION OF INSURANCE COMPANIES AND INSURANCE PRODUCERS 

[9:0:66,481] The objectives of this program are to: (1) prevent losses to policyholders, beneficiaries or the public due to the insolvency of 

[9:1:66,492] insurers; (2) prevent unlawful or unfair practices by insurers as defined by the Insurance Code; (3) ensure that property and 

[9:2:66,502] casualty insurance rates are not excessive, inadequate, unfairly discriminatory or otherwise in violation of the Insurance Code 

[9:3:66,512] and the California Code of Regulations; (4) review health insurance rates filed with CDI to determine whether they are 

[9:4:66,522] reasonable and attempt to get unreasonable rates lowered by insurers; and (5) ensure that applicants for insurance licenses, 

[9:5:66,532] and holders of insurance licenses, satisfy and maintain the qualifications for licensure. Through the Conservation and 

[9:6:66,543] Liquidation Office, the CDI administers the estates of insolvent and delinquent insurance companies. 

[9:7:66,560] 0525 - CONSUMER PROTECTION 

[10:0:66,578] The objectives of this program are to: (1) provide direct service to California consumers by protecting insurance policyholders 

[10:1:66,588] and other parties involved in insurance transactions against unfair or illegal practices with respect to claims handling, rating or 

[10:2:66,598] underwriting by insurers and (2) protect applicants and policyholders from discriminatory, unlawful or fraudulent practices or 

[10:3:66,609] incompetence relating to the sale of insurance and (3) oversee programs that benefit California's underserved communities. 

[10:4:66,626] 0530 - FRAUD CONTROL 

[11:0:66,644] The objective of this program is to protect the public from economic loss by actively investigating, arresting and referring for 

[11:1:66,654] prosecution or adjudication those who commit insurance fraud and other violations of the law. The program is primarily staffed 

[11:2:66,664] by sworn peace officers who conduct criminal investigations of insurance fraud and related criminal cases. The program also 

[11:3:66,675] implements the Insurance Frauds Prevention Act, which authorizes the Commissioner to initiate or intervene in whistle-blower 

[11:4:66,685] "qui tam" actions seeking civil penalties against perpetrators of insurance fraud. 

[11:5:66,703] 0535 - GENERAL FUND TAX COLLECTION AND COMPLIANCE 

[12:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[0:0:66,13] 2018-19 STATE BUDGET — LJE   184   LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[1:0:183,43] 0850   California State Lottery Commission 

[2:0:66,64] In 1984, Proposition 37 amended the California Constitution to authorize the establishment of a statewide lottery. As an 

[2:1:66,74] initiative statute, the California State Lottery Act (Act) of 1984 created the California State Lottery Commission and gave it 

[2:2:66,84] broad powers to oversee the operations of a statewide lottery. The purpose of the Act was to provide supplemental monies to 

[2:3:66,95] benefit public education. The Lottery is overseen by a five-person Commission appointed by the Governor and confirmed by 

[2:4:66,105] the State Senate. 

[3:0:66,125] The Act initially required that 50 percent of total annual revenues be returned to the public in the form of prizes and at least 34 

[3:1:66,135] percent of total revenues be allocated to the benefit of public education. No more than 16 percent of total revenues were to be 

[3:2:66,146] used for administrative costs. 

[4:0:66,166] In 2010, the Act was changed to allow the Lottery flexibility to pay out more money in prizes and reduce the administrative cost 

[4:1:66,176] limit to 13 percent of total revenues. Along with that flexibility, the new law requires the Lottery to meet minimum levels of 

[4:2:66,186] contribution to public education. Revenues to education are placed in a special fund, known as the California State Lottery 

[4:3:66,197] Education Fund, which holds revenues until they are allocated on a per capita basis, using prior year certified Average Daily 

[4:4:66,207] Attendance data, to the following categories: K-12 education, Community Colleges, the California State University, the 

[4:5:66,217] University of California, and other educational entities, including the California Schools for the Deaf and Blind. 

[5:0:66,237] In the 32 years since sales began in October 1985 through June 30, 2017, the California State Lottery has raised $32.5 billion 

[5:1:66,248] for public education, including $1.55 billion in FY 2016-17. 

[6:0:66,268] Because of the inherently variable nature of lottery ticket sales, revenue estimates for 2017-18 and 2018-19 cannot be made 

[6:1:66,278] with certainty. 

[7:0:66,305] LEGAL CITATIONS AND AUTHORITY 

[8:0:66,323] DEPARTMENT AUTHORITY 

[9:0:66,341] Government Code, Title 2, Division 1, Chapter 12.5 (Sections 8880-8880.72).

[1:0:169,43] 0855   California Gambling Control Commission 

[2:0:66,64] The mission of the California Gambling Control Commission (Commission) is to ensure integrity in California's gaming 

[2:1:66,74] environment. The Commission has jurisdiction over gambling establishments (cardrooms), third-party providers of proposition 

[2:2:66,84] player services, and certain aspects of Tribal casinos, pursuant to its authority under state law and Tribal-State Gaming 

[2:3:66,95] Compacts (Compacts). The Commission also has jurisdiction over gaming policies, regulations, criteria, and standards. 

[2:4:66,112] There are 89 licensed cardrooms in California over which the Commission has broad regulatory and adjudicatory authority. This 

[2:5:66,123] authority extends to the operation, concentration, and supervision of the cardrooms and all persons and things related to each 

[2:6:66,133] licensed establishment. 

[3:0:66,150] The Commission has fiduciary, regulatory, and administrative responsibilities related to Tribal gaming that include: (1) 

[3:1:66,161] distributing Tribal gaming revenues to Non-Compact, Non-Gaming and Limited-Gaming Tribes; (2) making suitability 

[3:2:66,171] determinations for key employees, vendors, and financial sources; and (3) serving as the administrator of the Indian Gaming 

[3:3:66,181] Revenue Sharing Trust Fund, Indian Gaming Special Distribution Fund, and the Tribal Nation Grant Fund.

[0:0:66,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE   2018-19 STATE BUDGET — LJE   191 

[1:0:209,43] 0860   State Board of Equalization 

[2:0:66,64] Prior to July 1, 2017, the State Board of Equalization (BOE) administered a variety of tax and fee programs including the Sales 

[2:1:66,74] and Use tax, as required by statute. The BOE also adjudicated appeals for these tax and fee programs including income tax. 

[3:0:66,95] Pursuant to Chapter 16, Statutes of 2017, (AB 102) most statutory   duties formerly assigned to the BOE were shifted to the 

[3:1:66,105] newly created California Department of Tax and Fee Administration (CDTFA) and the Office of Tax Appeals. 

[4:0:66,125] The BOE now handles only those duties assigned to it by the California Constitution, and   three statutorily prescribed property 

[4:1:66,135] tax programs.   The BOE's duties   primarily include assessing and allocating the property values of railroads and specified 

[5:0:66,146] utilities and businesses; adjudicating property tax appeals of taxable government owned property; overseeing the property tax 

[5:1:66,156] assessment practices of the 58 county assessors; adopting rules to clarify property tax laws; and administering the alcoholic 

[6:0:66,166] beverage excise tax and the insurance tax, including appeals. The BOE contracts with the CDTFA to administer the alcoholic 

[6:1:66,176] beverage tax and insurance tax program.

[1:0:226,43] 0870   Office of Tax Appeals 

[2:0:66,64] The Office of Tax Appeals (OTA) was established effective January 1, 2018, by Chapter 16, Statutes of 2017 (AB 102). The 

[2:1:66,74] OTA performs those tax appeal duties formerly assigned by statute to the State Board of Equalization, including personal 

[2:2:66,84] income tax appeals, franchise tax appeals, and sales and use tax appeals. The OTA's mission is to ensure tax appeals are 

[2:3:66,95] performed in a fair, transparent, consistent, equitable, and impartial manner. 

[3:0:66,115] The OTA has hearing offices in Sacramento, Fresno, and Los Angeles.

[3:1:66,83] 0620100 - EXECUTIVE DIVISION 

[4:0:66,104] The Executive Division provides leadership and direction to the OTA, and empowers staff to perform their duties fairly, 

[4:1:66,114] impartially, and in strict conformance with applicable statutes and regulations. 

[4:2:66,131] 0620200 - ADMINISTRATION DIVISION 

[5:0:66,149] The Administration Division ensures the efficient operation of the OTA by providing fiscal oversight and performing personnel 

[5:1:66,159] management, information technology, and business services functions. 

[5:2:66,177] 0620300 - HEARINGS DIVISION 

[6:0:66,195] The Hearings Division provides the venues in which taxpayers may appeal decisions rendered by the Franchise Tax Board and 

[6:1:66,205] the California Department of Tax and Fee Administration. Tax appeals are adjudicated by hearing panels consisting of three 

[6:2:66,215] Administrative Law Judges. 

[6:3:66,233] 0620400 - APPEALS BUREAU PROCESS DIVISION 

[7:0:66,251] The Appeals Bureau Process Division provides case research and support functions, and also sets the hearing calendar for tax 

[7:1:66,261] appeals.

[0:0:66,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE   2018-19 STATE BUDGET — LJE   211 

[1:0:236,43] 0890   Secretary of State 

[2:0:66,64] The Secretary of State, a constitutionally established office, is the chief elections officer of the state and is responsible for the 

[2:1:66,74] administration and enforcement of election laws. The Office also is responsible for administering and enforcing laws pertaining 

[2:2:66,84] to filing documents associated with corporations, limited liability companies, partnerships, limited partnerships, unincorporated 

[2:3:66,95] associations and pertaining to filing bonds and perfecting security agreements. In addition, the Office is responsible for 

[2:4:66,105] commissioning notaries public, enforcing the notary laws, and in conjunction with being the home of the State Archives, 

[3:0:66,115] administering a state records management program and preserving documents and records having historical significance. The 

[3:1:66,125] Secretary of State is the filing officer for lobbying and campaign registration and disclosure documents filed under the Political 

[3:2:66,135] Reform Act. The Secretary of State also operates the Safe at Home program, maintains the Domestic Partners and Advance 

[3:3:66,146] Health Care Directives registries, provides support functions for the Voting Modernization Board, and is home to the California 

[3:4:66,156] Museum. 

[4:0:66,174] Policy associated with the administration of the Office is accomplished through the divisions of Elections, Political Reform, 

[4:1:66,184] Business Programs, Archives, Information Technology, and Management Services.

[6:4:76,334] elections.

[7:1:76,360] research and development, purchase, or lease of hardware, software, and initial licensing for the replacement of voting 

[7:2:76,370] systems and technology in all 58 counties.   This funding will provide reimbursement to counties by matching county funds 

[7:3:76,380] spent on voting system replacement activities on a dollar-for-dollar basis, up to the maximum amount of funds allocated for 

[7:4:76,391] this purpose.

[4:0:66,228] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[5:0:66,246] 0700 - FILINGS AND REGISTRATIONS 

[6:0:66,267] The Business Programs Division is comprised of three primary areas: Business Entities, Uniform Commercial Code, and 

[6:1:66,277] Notary Public and Special Filings. 

[7:0:66,297] Business Entities examines, files, and maintains formation documents for new California business entities and qualifies out-of-

[7:1:66,307] state and international entities to conduct business in California; and also examines, files and maintains changes to the records 

[7:2:66,318] of domestic or qualified foreign business entities to ensure the business entities are properly formed, merged, converted, 

[7:3:66,328] amended, and dissolved in compliance with California law. Statements of Information containing key officers and contact 

[7:4:66,338] information are also processed and maintained for public and governmental use. 

[8:0:66,358] Uniform Commercial Code files certain financing statements allowing lending institutions to protect their financial interest in 

[8:1:66,369] personal property and established priority in case of debtor default or bankruptcy. Notices of judgment liens, attachment liens, 

[8:2:66,379] agricultural liens, and equipment liens are also filed in Uniform Commercial Code, as well as notices of various tax liens 

[8:3:66,389] submitted by state and federal taxing agencies such as the Franchise Tax Board and the Internal Revenue Service. 

[9:0:66,409] Notary Public and Special Filings appoints and commissions qualified notaries public for four- year terms, reviews and 

[9:1:66,420] approves notary public education courses, and certifies to the authenticity of signatures of notaries public and public officials on 

[9:2:66,430] documents for use in foreign countries. Special Filings include trademarks and service marks as well as surety bonds that 

[9:3:66,440] certain business entities are required to file, notices of joint power agreements, city and county charters, and claims for 

[9:4:66,450] successor-in-interest. This area also has disciplinary functions with regard to notaries public and immigration consultants. 

[10:0:66,471] The Advance Health Care Directive (AHCD) Registry maintains AHCDs, which allow a person to indicate his/her medical 

[10:1:66,481] treatment preferences if he/she cannot speak or make decisions for him/herself. The AHCD also may designate someone else 

[10:2:66,491] to make decisions regarding medical treatment. AHCDs and related information can be provided upon request to the 

[10:3:66,501] registrant's health care provider, public guardian, or legal representative. 

[11:0:66,522] The Domestic Partners Registry registers same-sex couples regardless of the age of the partners, and opposite-sex couples in 

[11:1:66,532] which one partner is at least 62 years old, as domestic partners in California. 

[12:0:66,552] The Victims of Corporate Fraud Compensation Fund provides limited restitution to victims of corporate fraud who are unable to 

[12:1:66,562] collect through other means on a judgment against a corporation for corporate fraud. 

[13:0:66,583] Safe at Home is a confidential mail forwarding program designed to assist victims of domestic violence and human trafficking, 

[13:1:66,593] sexual assault, stalking, or a reproductive healthcare employee, patient, or volunteer. Participants in the Safe at Home program 

[13:2:66,603] are provided with a free post office box to use as a substitute mailing address, thereby protecting their actual residential 

[13:3:66,613] address. Once enrolled into the program, Safe at Home serves as a liaison for those participants who qualify for additional 

[13:4:66,624] confidential services offered by various state and local agencies. 

[14:0:66,641] 0705 - ELECTIONS 

[15:0:66,659] The Secretary of State, as California's chief elections officer, has broad responsibility to administer the election process. This 

[15:1:66,669] program oversees all federal and state elections within California, maintaining a statewide database of all registered voters, 

[15:2:66,679] tracks and certifies ballot measures, certifies candidates, and certifies election results. In addition, the program oversees the 

[15:3:66,690] modernization of voting equipment and the improvement of current electoral database systems, enhancing the voting 

[15:4:66,700] processes, providing accessibility to voters, working to increase people's knowledge of the electoral system, and investigates 

[15:5:66,710] election related criminal violations as provided by California Elections Code. 

[16:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[1:0:382,24] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[2:0:201,38] 0890   Secretary of State - Continued 

[3:0:66,65] This program includes the campaign and lobbying registration and disclosure provisions of the Political Reform Act of 1974. 

[3:1:66,75] Under this law, the Office registers all levels of campaign committees, and reviews for compliance with statutory requirements 

[3:2:66,85] all state-level candidate and campaign recipient committees, major donors, slate mailer, political party, and independent 

[3:3:66,95] expenditure committees. The program registers lobbyists (including placement agents), lobbying firms and lobbyist employers, 

[3:4:66,106] reviews for compliance with statutory requirements the periodic financial disclosure reports filed by lobbying entities, and 

[3:5:66,116] publishes a Lobbying Directory on the Internet. This program also supports the electronic filing requirements of the Political 

[3:6:66,126] Reform Act and posts relevant registration and disclosure information to the Office's web site so the public can track where 

[3:7:66,136] campaign money is raised and spent and what lobbying activity is occurring in state government. A searchable database of 

[3:8:66,146] campaign and lobbying financial activity is also provided through the web site. 

[3:9:66,164] 0710 - ARCHIVES 

[4:0:66,182] The California State Archives establishes and administers a records management program that applies efficient and 

[4:1:66,192] economical management methods to the creation, utilization, maintenance, retention, preservation, and disposal of state 

[4:2:66,202] records and acquires, indexes, preserves, and provides reference access to irreplaceable historic materials from the three 

[4:3:66,212] branches of state government. The Archives acquires physical and electronic records with legal, administrative or evidentiary 

[4:4:66,223] value, or that have historical significance. Materials are transferred to the Archives for permanent preservation. 

[4:5:66,240] 9900 - ADMINISTRATION 

[5:0:66,258] This program develops and manages overall policy. It serves as the liaison to other state agencies, the Legislature, the federal 

[5:1:66,268] government, and other states' Secretaries of State. This program responds to constituent correspondence and requests from 

[5:2:66,278] the public and advocates in the Legislature, administers the Secretary's role as a member of the National Association of 

[5:3:66,289] Secretaries of State, in the management of task forces and advisory committees, in the implementation of new federal and 

[5:4:66,299] state statutory requirements, and coordinates and disseminates information about the Office and its initiatives to the public. It 

[5:5:66,309] also provides the Office with the necessary personnel, general administrative, budgeting, fiscal services, and information 

[5:6:66,319] technology expertise to plan, develop, implement, and operate innovative and effective business and information systems 

[5:7:66,329] solutions.

[1:0:243,43] 0950   State Treasurer 

[2:0:66,64] The State Treasurer, a constitutionally established office, provides banking services for state government with goals to 

[2:1:66,74] minimize interest and service costs and to maximize yield on investments. The Treasurer is responsible for the custody of all 

[2:2:66,84] monies and securities belonging to or held in trust by the state; investment of temporarily idle state monies; administration of 

[2:3:66,95] the sale of state bonds, their redemption and interest payments; and payment of warrants or checks drawn by the State 

[2:4:66,105] Controller and other state agencies.

[4:0:66,178] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[5:0:66,196] 0740010 - INVESTMENT SERVICES 

[6:0:66,213] The Investment Division is responsible for investment of state monies from the date of receipt through the date of redemption. 

[6:1:66,224] During the 2016-17 fiscal year, this Division handled 7,697 security investment transactions totaling $339.2 billion. The Pooled 

[6:2:66,234] Money Investment Board (PMIB) program accounted for 5,926 of these transactions totaling $295 billion; time deposits 

[6:3:66,244] accounted for 1,395 transactions totaling $34.2 billion. The remaining $10 billion is invested on behalf of the state's special 

[6:4:66,254] funds, such as those associated with the California Housing Finance Agency, the Department of Fish and Wildlife, the state's 

[6:5:66,264] retirement system, etc. The Division also administers the Local Agency Investment Fund (LAIF), a voluntary investment 

[6:6:66,275] program created to offer California local agencies greater access to the financial markets through the PMIB program. In the 

[6:7:66,285] 2016-17 fiscal year, 2,439 local agencies participated in LAIF, with deposits averaging $21.8 billion for the fiscal year. 

[7:0:66,303] 0740019 - CENTRALIZED TREASURY AND SECURITIES MANAGEMENT 

[8:0:66,320] In 1949, the California Legislature amended Government Code Section 16305 to create the Centralized Treasury System 

[8:1:66,330] (CTS) thereby requiring agencies of the State to deposit their money in trust with the Treasurer. The legislation also requires 

[8:2:66,341] the Treasurer to safeguard the money and make safe and prudent investments. In 1955, the Legislature created the PMIB, 

[8:3:66,351] giving the Board the responsibility to designate the amount of money available for the investment in securities, bank deposits, 

[8:4:66,361] and loans to the General Fund. 

[9:0:66,379] The Centralized Treasury and Securities Management Division (CTSMD) oversees all banking aspects of the CTS. The goal of 

[9:1:66,389] the CTSMD is to maximize the earning of interest consistent with safe and prudent treasury management, and to ensure that 

[9:2:66,399] the depository banks provide the state with proper and adequate security for the deposit of state monies. The Treasurer 

[9:3:66,409] maintains demand bank accounts with eight banks for the purpose of providing necessary statewide depository coverage for 

[9:4:66,420] the remittance of funds collected by the various state agencies. 

[10:0:66,437] The CTSMD 1) monitors the cash flow of all state funds, forecasts cash balances, revenue, expenditures, and the amounts 

[10:1:66,447] available for daily investments, 2) ensures accurate and timely agency deposits, 3) administers and executes the wire transfer 

[10:2:66,458] of funds, 4) reconciles state accounts with depository banks and redeems all state items submitted by presenting banks for 

[10:3:66,468] payments, 5) executes the clearance and income collection for state investments (excluding PERS and STRS) and securities 

[10:4:66,478] pledged to the state, 6) fulfills the clearance and settlement of securities pledged to the state for the time, demand, and other 

[10:5:66,488] state agency programs, and 7) is responsible for the safekeeping of securities and other personal property owned by or 

[10:6:66,498] pledged to the state. 

[11:0:66,516] 0740028 - PUBLIC FINANCE 

[12:0:66,534] The Public Finance Division is responsible for selling State of California general obligation bonds, commercial paper notes, 

[12:1:66,544] revenue anticipation notes, lease revenue bonds, revenue bonds, and any other indebtedness for all state agencies. The 

[12:2:66,554] Division provides debt administration services for most state bonds and is responsible for disseminating information to 

[12:3:66,564] bondholders through the Investor Relations Program. 

[13:0:66,582] The Division also assures compliance with federal tax laws applicable to state debt so there is no adverse consequences to 

[13:1:66,592] bondholders or increased borrowing costs to the state. The post-issuance tax compliance for various state debt includes 

[13:2:66,603] monitoring the use of bond proceeds, managing rebate and yield restriction compliance, coordinating necessary financial 

[13:3:66,613] disclosure, and ensuring proper record keeping. 

[14:0:66,630] 0740035 - ADMINISTRATION AND INFORMATION SERVICES 

[15:0:66,648] The Administration Division, Executive Office, and the Information Services Division provide executive direction and support 

[15:1:66,658] services to programs in the State Treasurer's Office. Services include budgeting, personnel, accounting, information systems, 

[15:2:66,669] business services, technical support, and production operations. 

[16:0:66,686] 9900 - ADMINISTRATION AND INFORMATION SERVICES 

[17:0:66,704] As of the 2017-18 Governor's Budget, this program has been consolidated with the Administration and Information Services 

[17:1:66,714] Program (0740035). 

[18:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:7:66,167] with scholarships. Students may have earned multiple $1,000 Governor's Scholars Awards and a one-time $2,500 Governor's 

[3:8:66,177] Distinguished Mathematics and Science Scholars Award for scores attained on tests taken in 2000, 2001, and 2002. GSP 

[3:9:66,187] became inoperative and was repealed after 2002. ScholarShare administers scholarship awards that were already earned. 

[3:10:66,205] 0790 - CALIFORNIA MEMORIAL SCHOLARSHIP PROGRAM 

[3:11:66,223] The California Memorial Scholarship Program provides scholarships to surviving dependents of California residents killed as a

[1:0:134,43] 0956   California Debt and Investment Advisory Commission 

[1:1:66,64] The mission of the California Debt and Investment Advisory Commission is to support and improve the practice of public 

[1:2:66,74] finance in California by providing responsive and reliable information, analysis, and training. The Commission assists California 

[1:3:66,84] state and local governments to monitor, issue, and manage public debt and to safely and prudently invest public funds. 

[1:4:66,102] The Commission consists of nine members including the State Treasurer, who serves as chairperson; the Governor or, upon

[1:6:66,123] Treasurer; two members of the Assembly appointed by the Speaker of the Assembly; and two members of the Senate

[0:0:66,13] 2018-19 STATE BUDGET — LJE   236   LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[1:0:165,43] 0959   California Debt Limit Allocation Committee 

[2:0:66,64] The California Debt Limit Allocation Committee's mission is to allocate tax-exempt, private activity bond authority for the State 

[2:1:66,74] of California. Private activity bonds may be used by the private sector for projects and programs that provide a public benefit, 

[2:2:66,84] such as the creation of affordable housing. 

[3:0:66,102] The federal government limits the total amount of tax-exempt private activity bond authority that can be issued on an annual 

[3:1:66,112] basis. Except for the Qualified Public Educational Facility Bond Program, the bond authority limit (debt limit) is calculated by 

[3:2:66,123] multiplying the state population by $100. California's limit totaled over $3.92 billion in 2017. (California's limit for the Qualified 

[3:3:66,133] Public Educational Facility Bond Program is calculated separately by multiplying the state population by $10, and totaled $392 

[3:4:66,143] million for 2017.) 

[4:0:66,161] The Committee's allocation of tax-exempt bond authority results in the issuance of bonds by cities, counties, joint powers 

[4:1:66,171] authorities and state agencies. The bonds are purchased and used by the private sector and are not an obligation of the state 

[4:2:66,181] or federal government. 

[5:0:66,199] The Committee administers 10 programs that are funded through the allocation and issuance of tax-exempt private activity 

[5:1:66,209] bonds, and is responsible for reallocation of Qualified Energy Conservation Bond (QECB) authority, originally provided to 

[5:2:66,219] qualified localities, but later waived back to the state. The QECB program was made available through the federal American 

[5:3:66,229] Recovery and Reinvestment Act of 2009. It provides tax incentives and lower borrowing costs for local governments and 

[5:4:66,240] private entities to promote job creation and economic recovery in areas particularly affected by employment decline. It also 

[5:5:66,250] facilitates renewable energy conservation programs and projects throughout the state. 

[6:0:66,267] The Committee is funded on a fee-supported basis and is comprised of the State Treasurer (Chairperson), the Governor, or 

[6:1:66,278] upon his designation, the Director of Finance, and the State Controller. 

[7:0:66,308] 3-YEAR EXPENDITURES AND POSITIONS 

[8:0:340,329] Positions   Expenditures 

[8:1:305,343] 2016-17   2017-18   2018-19   2016-17*   2017-18*   2018-19* 

[9:0:75,355] 0810   California Debt Limit Allocation Committee   9.6   9.6   9.6   $1,417   $1,587   $1,588 

[9:1:75,368] TOTALS, POSITIONS AND EXPENDITURES (All Programs)   9.6   9.6   9.6   $1,417   $1,587   $1,588

[2:0:130,38] 0959   California Debt Limit Allocation Committee - Continued 

[3:0:66,65] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[4:0:66,83] 0810 - CALIFORNIA DEBT LIMIT ALLOCATION COMMITTEE 

[5:0:66,101] To lower the cost of financing, specified entities can issue various bonds, including mortgage revenue bonds (MRBs) that 

[5:1:66,111] support below-market interest rate mortgage loans; and mortgage credit certificates (MCCs) that reduce a homebuyer's federal 

[5:2:66,121] tax liability. Below is a summary of each program: 

[6:0:66,139] Qualified Residential Rental Project Program - Tax-exempt housing revenue bonds lower the cost for developers of multifamily 

[6:1:66,149] rental housing to acquire land and construct new housing or purchase and rehabilitate existing housing. The developers 

[6:2:66,159] produce affordable rental housing for low-income households by reducing rental rates charged to these individuals and 

[6:3:66,170] families. Projects that receive an award of bond authority have the right to apply for non-competitive 

[7:0:66,187] four-percent tax credits. 

[8:0:66,205] Single-Family Housing Program - Tax-exempt MRBs or MCCs to assist first-time homebuyers with purchasing homes. 

[8:1:66,215] Homebuyers may purchase single-family homes, either freestanding or detached, condominiums, or townhouses. Program 

[8:2:66,225] participants must meet income limits and must purchase a home that falls within the program's purchase price limitations. 

[9:0:66,243] Home Improvement and Rehabilitation Bond Program - MRBs or MCCs to assist homeowners with home improvement 

[9:1:66,253] financing. Homeowners may improve single-family homes, either freestanding or detached, condominiums, or townhouses. 

[9:2:66,263] Program participants must meet income limits, and in certain cases, must own a home that falls within the program's 

[9:3:66,274] rehabilitation cost and age-of-home limitations. 

[10:0:66,291] Extra Credit Home Purchase Program - MRBs or MCCs to assist teachers, principals and other eligible school staff with 

[10:1:66,302] purchasing homes. 

[11:0:66,319] Industrial Development Bond Project Program - Small-Issue industrial development bonds are tax-exempt private activity bonds 

[11:1:66,329] issued to assist manufacturing facilities finance capital expenditures. These bonds offer interest rate savings to small and 

[11:2:66,340] midsize manufacturers in contrast to conventional loans, and help manufacturers retain and create new jobs within their 

[11:3:66,350] communities. 

[12:0:66,368] Exempt Facility Program - Tax-exempt private activity bonds issued to finance solid waste disposal and waste recycling 

[12:1:66,378] facilities. Loan interest rate savings enable the project owners to charge lower customer rates, while assisting communities 

[12:2:66,388] they serve to meet mandated requirements to protect and enhance the environment. 

[13:0:66,406] Student Loan Program - Tax-exempt private activity bonds issued for either financing direct loans to college students and their 

[13:1:66,416] parents, or purchasing bundles of already-originated loans on the secondary market. When used for direct lending programs, 

[13:2:66,426] tax-exempt bond allocation allows lenders to pass on interest rate savings to students via below-market interest rate loans. 

[14:0:66,444] Beginning Farmer Program - Tax-exempt private activity bonds issued to back below-market interest rate loans for agricultural 

[14:1:66,454] land, construction improvements, breeder livestock, and equipment for farmers. Eligibility, permissible items, and loan limits are 

[14:2:66,464] set by the United States Internal Revenue Code. Under the program, a conduit bond issuer applies to the Committee for an 

[14:3:66,474] allocation of Beginning Farmer Bonds. If approved, the issuer brings together farmers, financial institutions, and investors to 

[14:4:66,485] negotiate terms of a transaction. 

[15:0:66,502] Qualified Public Educational Facility Bond Program - Tax-exempt private activity bonds issued to finance the construction, 

[15:1:66,512] renovation, and furnishing of primary and secondary school facilities. 

[16:0:66,530] Qualified Energy Conservation Bond Program - Tax-exempt private activity bonds or governmental purpose taxable bonds 

[16:1:66,540] issued to finance a broad array of qualified conservation purposes such as capital expenditures, research facilities and 

[16:2:66,551] research grant programs, mass commuting facilities, public education campaigns and demonstration projects. When issued as 

[16:3:66,561] a governmental purpose taxable bond, QECBs provide the issuer with either a direct or tax credit interest subsidy. 

[17:0:66,601] DETAILED EXPENDITURES BY PROGRAM

[1:0:157,43] 0964   California Transportation Financing Authority 

[2:0:66,64] The California Transportation Financing Authority was established by Chapter 474, Statutes of 2009, to increase the 

[2:1:66,74] construction of new capacity or improvements for the state transportation system in a manner that will help achieve the state's 

[2:2:66,84] goals of reducing greenhouse gas, improving air quality, and conserving natural resources. 

[2:3:66,102] The Authority consists of seven members: the State Treasurer (Chairperson), the Director of the Department of Finance, the 

[2:4:66,112] State Controller, the Director of Transportation, the Executive Director of the California Transportation Commission, a local 

[2:5:66,123] agency representative appointed by the Senate Committee on Rules, and a local agency representative appointed by the 

[2:6:66,133] Speaker of the Assembly. 

[3:0:66,163] 3-YEAR EXPENDITURES AND POSITIONS

[6:1:66,272] DEPARTMENT AUTHORITY 

[6:2:66,290] Government Code, Division 3 (commencing with Section 64100) of Title 6.7. 

[7:0:66,327] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[7:1:66,345] 0820 - CALIFORNIA TRANSPORTATION FINANCING AUTHORITY 

[8:0:66,363] The Authority was established to assist transportation agencies in obtaining financing to develop transportation projects. To 

[8:1:66,373] meet this objective, statutes allow the Authority to issue, or to approve specified project sponsors to issue revenue bonds to 

[8:2:66,383] fund transportation projects backed in whole or in part, by various revenue streams of transportation funds (including toll 

[8:3:66,394] revenues). Statutes provide the authority to approve the imposition and collection of tolls on proposed transportation projects 

[8:4:66,404] under certain conditions. The planning, programming, and allocation of transportation projects are performed by the California 

[8:5:66,414] Transportation Commission and the California Department of Transportation, as appropriate.

[1:0:96,43] 0965   California Industrial Development Financing Advisory Commission 

[1:1:66,64] The California Industrial Development Financing Advisory Commission creates employment opportunities and supports local 

[1:2:66,74] economic development by approving local entities' issuance of industrial development bonds. These bonds provide 

[1:3:66,84] manufacturers with a low-cost financing option to build, expand, or equip their facilities in California. The Commission 

[1:4:66,95] independently reviews bond applications to ensure compliance with federal tax law and state law, provides technical assistance 

[1:5:66,105] to bond issuers, and approves the bond sales by local authorities. 

[2:0:66,123] The State Treasurer serves as chairperson of the Commission. The other members are the Director of the Department of 

[2:1:66,133] Finance, the State Controller, the Director of the Governor's Office of Business and Economic Development, and the 

[2:2:66,143] Commissioner of the Department of Business Oversight.

[7:0:66,391] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[8:0:66,409] 0830 - CALIFORNIA INDUSTRIAL DEVELOPMENT FINANCING ADVISORY COMMISSION 

[9:0:66,426] The core program area is the Industrial Development Bond Program, under which the Commission serves as the mandatory 

[9:1:66,437] approval agency for the local bond issuers. Once potential borrowers (manufacturers and processing companies) apply through 

[9:2:66,447] their local government agency for low cost tax-exempt financing, the Commission carefully reviews the public benefits 

[9:3:66,457] generated by the proposed project, particularly those benefits associated with job creation, and determines whether these 

[9:4:66,467] benefits will significantly outweigh any detrimental public effects. Eligible capital expenditures include the acquisition of land, 

[9:5:66,477] building construction, building renovation, and the purchase of machinery and equipment. Bond proceeds also can be used to 

[9:6:66,488] cover the cost of architects, engineers, attorneys, permits, and the cost of bond issuance. The local issuer can be a city, county, 

[9:7:66,498] economic development authority, or a joint power authority.

[1:0:166,43] 0968   California Tax Credit Allocation Committee 

[2:0:66,64] The mission of the California Tax Credit Allocation Committee is to fairly allocate federal and state tax credits to create and 

[2:1:66,74] maintain safe, quality affordable rental housing for low-income households in California. The Committee does this by forming 

[2:2:66,84] partnerships with developers, investors and public entities. 

[3:0:66,102] The Committee assists development of rental housing projects for low-income families and monitors these projects for years 

[3:1:66,112] after they are placed in service to ensure compliance with income and rent restrictions related to the tax credit programs of the 

[3:2:66,123] Committee. Coordinating with state and local housing fund providers, and with private investors, the Committee helps to create 

[3:3:66,133] and maintain a supply of quality, affordable housing. 

[4:0:66,150] The Committee consists of seven members, including three voting members and four advisors. The voting members are the 

[4:1:66,161] Treasurer (Chairperson of the Committee), the Governor (who may choose the Director of Finance as his or her 

[5:0:66,171] representative), and the State Controller. The non-voting (advisory) members are the Director of Housing and Community 

[5:1:66,181] Development, the Executive Director of the California Housing Finance Agency, and two local government representatives. 

[5:2:66,191] One local representative is associated with a city government and appointed by the Speaker of the Assembly. The other is a 

[5:3:66,201] county government representative appointed by the Senate Rules Committee. 

[6:0:66,232] 3-YEAR EXPENDITURES AND POSITIONS 

[7:0:340,253] Positions   Expenditures 

[7:1:305,266] 2016-17   2017-18   2018-19   2016-17*   2017-18*   2018-19*

[0:0:66,24] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[1:0:410,24] 2018-19 STATE BUDGET — LJE   245 

[2:0:131,38] 0968   California Tax Credit Allocation Committee - Continued 

[3:0:66,65] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[3:1:66,83] 0840 - CALIFORNIA TAX CREDIT ALLOCATION COMMITTEE 

[3:2:66,101] The Federal Program 

[4:0:66,119] Congress authorized the federal Low-Income Housing Tax Credit (LIHTC) program in 1986. It replaced traditional housing tax 

[4:1:66,129] incentives, such as accelerated depreciation, with a tax credit that enables developers of affordable rental housing to raise 

[4:2:66,139] project equity through the "sale" of tax benefits to investors. The federal per-capita tax credit ceiling is calculated annually and 

[4:3:66,149] indexed for inflation. For 2018, each state has an annual housing credit ceiling of $2.40 per state resident. Additionally, each 

[4:4:66,159] state may qualify annually for a share of unused credits in a national pool. 

[4:5:66,177] Two types of federal tax credits are available, generally referred to as 9-percent and 4-percent credits. These terms refer to the 

[4:6:66,187] percentage of a project's "qualified basis" an investor in LIHTC projects may deduct from his or her federal income tax liability 

[4:7:66,197] annually in each of ten years. A project's qualified basis is the portion of the total project cost used to calculate the amount of 

[4:8:66,208] the tax credit and typically excludes non-depreciable costs such as the purchase of land. The affordable housing project 

[4:9:66,218] sponsor uses or sells ten times the project's tax-credit allocation amount because investors can take the annual tax credit each 

[4:10:66,228] year for a ten-year period. 

[4:11:66,246] The State LIHTC Program 

[5:0:66,263] In 1987, recognizing the high cost of housing in California, the Legislature authorized the state's program to augment the 

[5:1:66,274] federal program. Housing projects receiving (or that have previously received) an allocation of federal tax credits may apply for 

[5:2:66,284] an allocation of state tax credits. Projects serving farmworkers may apply for state tax credits regardless of their federal tax 

[5:3:66,294] credit status. 

[6:0:66,312] The annual state credit ceiling is indexed for inflation, and for 2017, was $96.5 million. Investors take the state tax credit 

[6:1:66,322] annually over a four-year period in contrast to the ten-year federal period. 

[6:2:66,340] Tax-Exempt Bond Financed Program 

[7:0:66,357] Developments financed with tax-exempt bond proceeds may also receive federal tax credit. The sponsors of such projects 

[7:1:66,368] must apply to the Committee and must conform to applicable federal and state statutory and regulatory requirements, but there 

[7:2:66,378] is no annual cap on the amount of credit that may be awarded by the state to such developments. The annual credit available 

[7:3:66,388] is based on approximately 4 percent of the qualified basis of the development. The qualified basis includes those costs 

[7:4:66,398] attributable to the units that will be income and rent restricted for a minimum of 30 years. 

[7:5:66,416] Under federal law, credit projects must remain affordable for at least 30 years; however, California's program generally requires 

[7:6:66,426] maintaining affordability for 55 years. Land use agreements are recorded against each credit project to ensure compliance. 

[7:7:66,444] Farmworker Housing Program 

[8:0:66,461] Chapter 521, Statutes of 2008, folded the previous Farmworker Housing Assistance Tax Credit program into the general state 

[8:1:66,472] LIHTC program as a $500,000 annual set-aside in support of farmworker housing. Pursuant to Chapter 372, Statutes of 2017, 

[8:2:66,482] these credits differ from other state credits in that the credit percentage for tax-exempt bond financed projects is higher and 

[8:3:66,492] projects may receive state credits even if they receive an increase in their federal tax credit basis. 

[9:0:66,533] DETAILED EXPENDITURES BY PROGRAM

[0:0:66,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE   2018-19 STATE BUDGET — LJE   249 

[1:0:88,43] 0971   California Alternative Energy and Advanced Transportation Financing 

[1:1:279,57] Authority 

[2:0:66,78] The California Alternative Energy and Advanced Transportation Financing Authority was established by Chapter 908, Statutes 

[2:1:66,89] of 1980, to promote the prompt and efficient development of energy sources which are renewable or which more efficiently 

[2:2:66,99] utilize and conserve scarce energy resources. Recognizing the importance of developing a secure energy future to protect the 

[2:3:66,109] environment and ensure economic stability, the Authority advances the state's goals of reducing greenhouse gas emissions by 

[2:4:66,119] providing financing options to assist industries in increasing the deployment of sustainable and renewable energy sources, 

[2:5:66,129] implementing measures that increase the efficiency of the use of energy, creating high quality employment opportunities, and 

[2:6:66,140] lessening the state's dependence on fossil fuels. To support these goals, the Authority provides financing and credit 

[2:7:66,150] enhancements to promote the establishment of facilities that use alternative methods and sources of energy, and facilities 

[2:8:66,160] needed for the development and commercialization of advanced transportation technologies. 

[3:0:66,178] The Authority consists of five members: the State Treasurer, who serves as the chairperson, the State Controller, the Director 

[3:1:66,188] of the Department of Finance, the Chairperson of the State Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission, 

[3:2:66,198] and the President of the Public Utilities Commission. 

[4:0:66,229] 3-YEAR EXPENDITURES AND POSITIONS

[4:0:66,114] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[5:0:66,132] 0850 - ALTERNATIVE ENERGY AND ADVANCED TRANSPORTATION FINANCING AUTHORITY 

[5:1:66,150] Financial assistance can take the form of loans, loan loss reserves, interest rate reductions, proceeds of bonds issued by the 

[5:2:66,160] authority, bond insurance, loan guarantees or other credit enhancements, contributions of money, or a combination thereof, as 

[5:3:66,170] determined and approved by resolution of the Authority. 

[6:0:66,188] In 2010, the Authority launched a sales and use tax exclusion (STE) program for advanced transportation and alternative 

[6:1:66,198] source manufacturing in California (Chapter 10, Statutes of 2010) and subsequently, Chapter 677, Statutes of 2012, expanded 

[6:2:66,208] the STE program to include advanced manufacturing, with an annual cap of $100 million available for STE awards. Chapter 

[6:3:66,218] 768, Statutes of 2015, further expanded the program to also include equipment primarily used to process recycled feedstock 

[6:4:66,229] and equipment that primarily utilizes recycled feedstock in the production of another product. The objective of this program is to 

[6:5:66,239] promote the creation of California-based manufacturing, businesses, and jobs that are aimed at reducing greenhouse gases, 

[6:6:66,249] air and water pollution, and energy consumption. 

[7:0:66,267] Legislation in 2010 (Chapter 15, Statutes of 2010), 2011 (Chapter 9, Statutes of 2011, First Extraordinary Session), and 2013 

[7:1:66,277] (Chapter 356, Statutes of 2013), authorized financial assistance to encourage energy efficiency retrofits. The Authority 

[7:2:66,287] developed a loan loss reserve program to assist financial institutions in making loans to homeowners in the state for residential 

[7:3:66,297] whole house energy efficiency and renewable energy projects, and developed a risk mitigation program for residential Property 

[7:4:66,308] Assessed Clean Energy programs in California. 

[8:0:66,325] Most recently, the Budget Act of 2014 authorized administration of the California Hub for Energy Efficiency Financing on behalf 

[8:1:66,335] of, and in collaboration with, the California Public Utilities Commission. These energy efficiency financing pilot programs will 

[8:2:66,346] employ various types of credit enhancements to encourage and leverage private capital lending (in both the residential and 

[8:3:66,356] commercial sectors) for energy efficiency improvements needed to help achieve California's energy efficiency goals. 

[9:0:66,396] DETAILED EXPENDITURES BY PROGRAM 

[10:0:420,418] 2016-17*   2017-18*   2018-19* 

[10:1:102,431] PROGRAM REQUIREMENTS

[10:3:102,451] FINANCING AUTHORITY 

[10:4:102,463] State Operations:

[1:0:151,43] 0974   California Pollution Control Financing Authority 

[2:0:66,64] California Pollution Control Financing Authority is committed to stimulating local economies in California by making capital more 

[2:1:66,74] accessible to small businesses, and delivering innovative financing for projects that protect and restore the environment. The 

[2:2:66,84] Authority was created to assist the solid waste, recycling, water, and wastewater industries through its Pollution Control Tax-

[2:3:66,95] Exempt Bond Program. Over the last twenty years, the Authority has evolved to provide financing assistance to California's 

[2:4:66,105] small business sector through its California Capital Access Program (CalCAP), and to promote the reuse and redevelopment of 

[2:5:66,115] brownfields through the California Recycle Underutilized Sites Program (CALReUSE). 

[3:0:66,133] The Authority consists of the State Treasurer (Chairperson), the State Controller, and the Director of the Department of 

[3:1:66,143] Finance. 

[4:0:66,173] 3-YEAR EXPENDITURES AND POSITIONS

[12:0:66,660] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[13:0:66,679] 0860 - POLLUTION CONTROL TAX-EXEMPT BOND PROGRAM 

[14:0:66,696] The objective of this program is to provide bond financing to California businesses, irrespective of company size, for the 

[14:1:66,707] acquisition, construction, or installation of qualified pollution control, waste disposal, water furnishing, wastewater treatment, 

[15:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

# === PAGE 255 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,24] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[1:0:410,24] 2018-19 STATE BUDGET — LJE   253 

[2:0:116,38] 0974   California Pollution Control Financing Authority - Continued 

[3:0:66,65] waste recovery facilities, and the acquisition and installation of new equipment. Tax-exempt financing issued by the Authority 

[3:1:66,75] assists municipalities in complying with waste diversion mandates of the California Integrated Waste Management Act of 1989. 

[3:2:66,85] As of June 30, 2017, bonds totaling approximately $15.3 billion have been issued by the Authority. 

[4:0:66,103] 0865 - CALIFORNIA CAPITAL ACCESS PROGRAM FOR SMALL BUSINESSES 

[5:0:66,121] The objective of this program is to encourage banks and other financial institutions to make loans to small businesses. CalCAP 

[5:1:66,131] for Small Businesses is a form of loan loss recovery that may provide up to 100 percent coverage on certain loan defaults. By 

[5:2:66,141] participating in CalCAP, lenders have available to them a proven financing mechanism to meet the financing needs of 

[5:3:66,151] California's small businesses. The program works through the creation of a loan loss-reserve fund for each participating 

[5:4:66,161] financial institution. The Authority funds this reserve together with the financial institution and the borrower. Additional 

[5:5:66,172] incentives are provided to lend to businesses located in specified communities. The reserve serves to reduce loan-loss risk, 

[5:6:66,182] allowing banks to lend to targeted California small businesses. Chapter 731, Statutes of 2010, appropriated $6 million General 

[5:7:66,192] Fund to CalCAP to increase lending efforts. As of June 30, 2017, approximately $1.877 million of the $6 million remained in the 

[5:8:66,202] State-funded program account to assist small businesses. 

[6:0:66,220] In addition, California was awarded an allocation of federal funds in the amount of $168.6 million from the federal State Small 

[6:1:66,230] Business Credit Initiative (SSBCI). The Authority and the Governor's Office of Business and Economic Development (Go-BIZ) 

[6:2:66,240] equally share this allocation of $84.3 million each. These funds have enabled the Authority to expand the availability of its 

[6:3:66,251] CalCAP program statewide. The Authority also launched the Collateral Support Program to assist more small businesses and 

[6:4:66,261] utilize the SSBCI federal funds. Funds were allocated to California in three installments based on program progress. The 

[6:5:66,271] Authority received $27 million in March 2011, $27 million in December 2013, and $29 million in August 2015. The Authority was 

[6:6:66,281] allocated an additional $321,000 in December 2016, the final receipt of federal funds under the SSBCI program. As of June 30, 

[6:7:66,291] 2017, approximately $900,000 remained in the federally-funded program account to assist small businesses under the CalCAP 

[6:8:66,302] loan loss reserve program. Approximately $5 million remained in the federally-funded program account for the Collateral 

[6:9:66,312] Support Program. Funds are being recaptured to sustain the programs. 

[7:0:66,329] Utilizing the CalCAP model, the Authority has partnered with the Air Resources Board (ARB) to assist diesel truck and 

[7:1:66,340] equipment owners in meeting new clean air requirements by directing a total of $107.3 million in Air Quality Improvement 

[7:2:66,350] Program funds towards the purchase or retrofit of diesel trucks to comply with ARB's Statewide Truck and Bus Rule. The 

[7:3:66,360] Authority has also partnered with the California Energy Commission to implement a $2 million pilot program to provide credit 

[7:4:66,370] enhancements and rebates to small business borrowers who install electric vehicle charging stations. Similarly, the Authority is 

[7:5:66,380] partnering with the Department of Resources, Recycling, and Recovery to assist with the financing of important state recycling 

[7:6:66,391] objectives. 

[8:0:66,408] 0870 - CALIFORNIA RECYCLE UNDERUTILIZED SITES PROGRAM 

[9:0:66,426] The CALReUSE program assists with the reuse and redevelopment of underutilized properties with real or perceived 

[9:1:66,436] contamination issues. CALReUSE addresses a funding and information gap in the development of contaminated properties 

[9:2:66,446] (brownfields) to help bring these properties into productive reuse. Beginning in 2000, the CALReUSE Assessment Program 

[9:3:66,457] provided forgivable loans of up to $300,000, which was later increased to $500,000 per brownfield site. The funds assisted with 

[9:4:66,467] brownfield site assessment and characterization, technical assistance, and the development of remedial action plans. Eligible 

[9:5:66,477] projects included sites with potential beneficial reuse not currently redeveloped due to lack of information about real or 

[9:6:66,487] perceived contamination, uncertainty about clean-up costs, or concerns regarding time frames and the regulatory process. 

[10:0:66,505] Additionally, Proposition 1C, the Housing and Emergency Shelter Trust Fund Act of 2006, included provisions to fund 

[11:0:66,515] brownfield cleanup that resulted in the expansion of the CALReUSE Program. The CALReUSE Remediation Program received 

[11:1:66,525] $60 million in Proposition 1C funds and provided up to $5 million per project in loans and grants for the purpose of brownfield 

[11:2:66,536] cleanup that promotes affordable infill residential and mixed-used development. All CALReUSE Program funds have been 

[11:3:66,546] awarded. The CALReUSE Program's $60 million investment in cleanup of contaminated sites will result in 5,618 housing units 

[11:4:66,556] in 30 California communities. 

[12:0:66,574] 0875 - ALTERNATIVE FINANCING PROGRAMS 

[13:0:66,591] Under its authority to provide alternative methods of financing facilities for pollution control, clean water supply and alternative 

[13:1:66,602] energy production, the Authority is developing alternative financing models to assist sister agencies with achieving the state's 

[13:2:66,612] environmental goals. The Authority is authorized to receive and accept contributions from other sources including federal and 

[13:3:66,622] state agencies to carry out the purposes of its statutory authority. 

[14:0:66,640] 0876 - CALIFORNIA CAPITAL ACCESS PROGRAM FOR COMPLIANCE WITH THE AMERICANS WITH DISABLITIES ACT 

[15:0:66,657] The objective of this program, created by Chapter 787, Statutes of 2015, is to help small businesses comply with the federal 

[15:1:66,668] Americans with Disabilities Act and increase access for their employees and customers by encouraging lenders and other 

[15:2:66,678] financial institutions to make loans to very small businesses to finance tenant improvements. The Legislature provided the 

[15:3:66,688] Authority an appropriation of $10 million effective January 1, 2016, to administer a credit enhancement program following the 

[15:4:66,698] CalCAP model and create loan loss reserve accounts for lenders that enroll qualifying loans into the program. 

[16:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,65] 0877 - CALIFORNIA SEISMIC SAFETY CAPITAL ACCESS LOAN PROGRAM 

[3:1:66,83] The California Seismic Safety Capital Access Loan Program, authorized by Chapter 32, Statutes of 2016, provided a one-time 

[3:2:66,93] allocation of $10 million to create an accessible loan program to qualified small businesses and residential property owners 

[3:3:66,103] (including multi-unit dwellings and mobile homes registered by the California Department of Housing and Community 

[3:4:66,113] Development) for costs related to seismic retrofitting. The Program encourages banks and other financial institutions to 

[3:5:66,123] participate by creating loan loss reserve accounts for lenders that enroll qualifying loans into the program. The loans to small 

[3:6:66,134] businesses and residential property owners are for financing seismic retrofits of real property to reduce losses and mitigate 

[3:7:66,144] seismic damage.

[1:0:154,43] 0977   California Health Facilities Financing Authority 

[2:0:66,64] The mission of the California Health Facilities Financing Authority is to help eligible and credit worthy nonprofit and public 

[2:1:66,74] health facilities reduce their cost of capital, and promote health care improvement and cost containment objectives by providing 

[2:2:66,84] cost-effective tax-exempt bond, low-cost loan, and direct grant programs. 

[3:0:66,105] The Authority was established by Chapter 1033, Statutes of 1979 (AB 1558), and consists of nine members: the State 

[3:1:66,115] Treasurer, the Director of the Department of Finance, the State Controller, two public members appointed by the Senate, two 

[3:2:66,125] public members appointed by the Assembly, and two public members appointed by the Governor.

[0:0:66,24] 2018-19 STATE BUDGET — LJE   258 

[1:0:382,24] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[2:0:119,38] 0977   California Health Facilities Financing Authority - Continued 

[3:0:66,84] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[4:0:66,102] 0880 - CHILDREN'S HOSPITAL PROGRAM 

[5:0:66,123] In 2004, California voters approved Proposition 61, which established the Children's Hospital Program. In 2008, Proposition 3 

[5:1:66,133] established the second Children's Hospital Program. The purpose of both programs is to improve the health and welfare of 

[5:2:66,143] California's critically ill children by providing a stable and ready source of funds for capital improvement projects for children's 

[5:3:66,153] hospitals. Children's Hospital Program grant funding is derived from the issuance of general obligation bonds, up to $750 

[5:4:66,164] million under Proposition 61 and $980 million under Proposition 3. 

[6:0:66,181] 0885 - HEALTH FACILITIES GRANTS AND LOANS 

[7:0:66,202] The Authority serves as a conduit provider of tax-exempt bonds and a direct provider of financial assistance to health 

[7:1:66,212] institutions. 

[8:0:66,232] Tax-Exempt Bond Program 

[9:0:66,253] Bonds issued under this conduit program are not a debt, liability, or a pledge of the full faith and credit of the taxing power of 

[9:1:66,263] the state or any of its political subdivisions. The full faith and credit of the participating institutions are pledged for repayment of 

[9:2:66,273] the bonds. To qualify for funding, the proposed project must be a health facility, operated by a private nonprofit corporation or 

[9:3:66,283] association, city, city and county, county, or hospital district. 

[10:0:66,304] Lifeline Grant Program 

[11:0:66,324] Chapter 52, Statutes of 2017 (SB 97) authorized the Authority to approve grants of up to $20 million dollars from the HELP II 

[11:1:66,334] Loan Program subfund account. The Lifeline Grant Program will assist small and rural health facilities, including community-

[11:2:66,344] based clinics, that may be adversely affected financially by a reduction or elimination of federal government assistance and that 

[11:3:66,355] have little to no access to working capital. Grants must be awarded by June 30, 2020. 

[12:0:66,375] HELP II Loan Program 

[13:0:66,395] The Authority administers the Healthcare Expansion Loan Program II (HELP II) to assist small and rural health facilities in 

[13:1:66,406] obtaining financing for their capital needs. The HELP II Program provides two percent and three percent fixed interest rate 

[13:2:66,416] loans of up to $1,500,000 to California's nonprofit small and rural health facilities in an efficient, timely, and cost effective 

[13:3:66,426] manner. The two percent interest rate applies to loans approved from April 30, 2015 - April 30, 2017, and is effective for the life 

[13:4:66,436] of the loan. Refinancing loans are not eligible for the two percent interest rate. Refinancing loans are eligible up to $1,000,000. 

[13:5:66,446] HELP II loans may be used to purchase or construct new facilities, remodel or renovate existing facilities, purchase equipment 

[13:6:66,457] and/or furnishings, and refinance existing debt. 

[14:0:66,477] California Health Access Model Program 

[15:0:66,497] Chapter 23, Statutes of 2012 (AB 1467) authorized the California Health Access Model Program (CHAMP), a grant program 

[15:1:66,508] intended to support innovative methods of delivering health care services and improve health outcomes for vulnerable 

[15:2:66,518] populations and communities by bringing services to individuals where they live or congregate. 

[16:0:66,536] 0890 - MENTAL HEALTH WELLNESS GRANTS 

[17:0:66,556] Chapter 34, Statutes of 2013 (SB 82), the Investment in Mental Health Wellness Act of 2013 and related legislation provided 

[17:1:66,566] $142.5 million in one-time General Fund, and $4 million in ongoing Mental Health Services Act funding to provide grants for 

[17:2:66,576] community based mental health crisis support, which may also be eligible for matching federal funding. 

[18:0:66,597] The Act established a grant program to disburse funds to California counties or to their nonprofit or public agency designates 

[18:1:66,607] for the purpose of developing mental health crisis support programs. Specifically, funds will increase capacity for client 

[18:2:66,617] assistance and services in crisis intervention, crisis stabilization, crisis residential treatment, rehabilitative mental health 

[18:3:66,627] services, and mobile crisis support teams. The grants will support capital improvement, expansion and limited start-up costs. 

[19:0:66,648] Children and Youth Mental Health Wellness Grants 

[20:0:66,668] With the passage of Chapter 30, Statutes of 2016 (SB 833), the Children and Youth Investment in Mental Health Wellness 

[20:1:66,678] Grant Program was created to increase mental health services for children and youth 21 years of age and under to develop a 

[20:2:66,689] complete continuum of crisis services. Working with counties, the program will fund facility acquisition, construction/renovation, 

[20:3:66,699] equipment acquisition, and applicable startup or expansion costs to provide mental health services for children and youth as 

[20:4:66,709] well as provide family care throughout the state. 

[21:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[4:0:66,106] Community Services Infrastructure Grants 

[5:0:66,126] Chapter 33, Statutes of 2016 (SB 843) creates the Community Services Infrastructure competitive grant program to expand 

[5:1:66,136] community alternatives to jail and prison. The program seeks to expand access to jail and prison diversion programs and 

[5:2:66,146] services for those with mental health illness, substance use disorders, or who have suffered from trauma. Working with 

[5:3:66,157] counties and/or cities, the grant program will fund facility acquisition, construction/renovation, equipment acquisition, and 

[5:4:66,167] applicable startup or expansion costs for facilities that provide services to this population. 

[6:0:66,187] Funded with a one-time General Fund appropriation in fiscal year 2017-18 of $67,500,000 with $1,687,000 for administrative 

[6:1:66,197] costs.

[13:0:66,626] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[13:1:66,645] 0895 - CALIFORNIA ABLE ACT BOARD 

[14:0:66,662] On December 19, 2014, President Obama signed the Stephen Beck Jr., Achieving a Better Life Experience Act of 2014, which 

[14:1:66,672] allows individuals who became blind or disabled before reaching age 26, to create tax-free savings accounts. Individuals can 

[14:2:66,683] make nondeductible cash contributions to an ABLE account in the name of a designated beneficiary with tax-free earnings. 

[14:3:66,693] ABLE account distributions are also not included in the beneficiary's income, as long as they are used for qualified disability 

[14:4:66,703] expenses. Chapter 774, Statutes of 2015 (AB 449), implements the ABLE Act in California, and directs the Board, with the 

[14:5:66,713] State Treasurer as Chair, to administer the California ABLE Program (the Program) accounts on behalf of eligible individuals. 

[15:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[2:1:66,74] and develop in an environmentally and economically sound manner, the coastal and inland urban waterfront areas of the state, 

[2:2:66,84] in cooperation with local governments. The Authority consists of five members: the State Treasurer (Chairperson), the Director

[8:0:66,366] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[9:0:66,384] 0900 - The Authority was established to issue up to $650 million in conduit financing to make loans or acquire title to property 

[9:1:66,394] and to underwrite or undertake directly a variety of urban waterfront development projects within the coastal zone, the 

[9:2:66,405] Sacramento-Yolo Port District, the Stockton Port District, and those metropolitan statistical areas meeting specified conditions. 

[9:3:66,415] The Federal Tax Reform Act of 1986 requires that bonds authorized by the Authority generally be within an allocation from the 

[9:4:66,425] state's ''private activity'' bond limit in order for the bonds to be federally tax-exempt, unless the issuer qualifies as a private, 

[9:5:66,435] nonprofit business. 

[10:0:66,453] Financing is available for both publicly and privately sponsored projects that provide visitor-serving facilities, waterfront-

[10:1:66,463] dependent industries, public recreation, and erosion control facilities. The State Coastal Conservancy must approve both the 

[10:2:66,473] specific project and a master plan for urban waterfront restoration before any project can obtain the Authority approval for 

[10:3:66,483] revenue bond financing. The Authority has sold $3.33 million in revenue bonds to date.

[2:1:66,74] districts, community college districts, county offices of education, and charter schools. The State Treasurer serves as chair of

[5:0:66,215] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[5:1:66,233] 0918 - SMART BONDS PROGRAM 

[6:0:66,253] Chapter 1438, Statutes of 1985, as amended by Chapter 598, Statutes of 1987, established CSFA and authorized the issuance 

[6:1:66,263] of revenue bonds or other debt instruments. The proceeds from the sale of the bonds were available for loans to schools and 

[6:2:66,274] community college districts to assist with the acquisition of equipment and new school sites, construction of new facilities, 

[6:3:66,284] reconstruction of existing facilities, capital improvements, and to provide working capital. Chapter 325, Statutes of 2006 (AB 

[6:4:66,294] 2717), allows CSFA to issue debt on behalf of California charter schools for capital improvements and working capital needs. 

[6:5:66,312] 0920 - CHARTER SCHOOL FACILITIES PROGRAM 

[7:0:66,329] Chapter 935, Statutes of 2002, as amended by Chapter 587, Statutes of 2003, created the Charter School Facilities Program 

[7:1:66,340] (CSFP), which provides funding for the new construction, renovation or rehabilitation of charter school facilities. CSFA and the 

[7:2:66,350] Office of Public School Construction jointly administer the program. CSFP was funded through the Kindergarten-University 

[7:3:66,360] Public Education Facilities Bond Acts of 2002, 2004, 2006, and 2016, for a total of $1.4 billion. The program provides a 50 

[7:4:66,370] percent state subsidy for the charter school facilities project costs, with the balance of the project costs being repaid (to the 

[7:5:66,380] state) by the charter school in the form of a long-term lease. Recipient charter schools must provide site-based instruction, be 

[7:6:66,391] deemed financially sound by CSFA, and meet other program eligibility requirements. 

[7:7:66,408] 0925 - STATE CHARTER SCHOOL FACILITIES INCENTIVE GRANTS PROGRAM 

[8:0:66,426] In 2004, 2009, and 2014, CSFA was awarded grants under the United States Department of Education's State Charter School 

[8:1:66,436] Facilities Incentive Grants Program (CFDA 84.282D) to provide California charter schools with assistance for facilities costs. 

[8:2:66,446] Grant funds reimburse a charter school's costs for rent, lease, mortgage or debt service payments for existing or new facilities 

[8:3:66,457] or the costs of acquiring land and/or constructing or renovating a facility. Grants are awarded to charter schools based on 

[8:4:66,467] preference points allotted by the percentage of low-income students, percentage of overcrowding, not-for-profit status, and 

[8:5:66,477] demonstrated student performance. Schools cannot use program funds to supplant state funding. 

[8:6:66,495] 0930 - CHARTER SCHOOL FACILITY GRANT PROGRAM 

[9:0:66,512] The Charter School Facility Grant Program provides Proposition 98 General Fund grants to assist charter schools with rent and 

[9:1:66,523] lease costs. This program targets schools and communities with significant populations of economically disadvantaged 

[9:2:66,533] students. Eligible charter schools are provided up to $1,145 per unit of eligible average daily attendance, not to exceed 75 

[9:3:66,543] percent of their total annual facilities rent or lease costs. 

[9:4:66,561] 0935 - CHARTER SCHOOL REVOLVING LOAN FUND 

[10:0:66,578] Charter 1010, Statutes of 1976 established the Charter School Revolving Loan Fund in the State Treasury to provide loans of 

[10:1:66,589] up to $250,000 to new charter schools to assist them in meeting the purposes of the school's approved charter. The program is 

[10:2:66,599] available to any charter school that is not a conversion of an existing public school, and preference is given to schools that 

[10:3:66,609] have not completed their full charter term.

[1:0:168,43] 0989   California Educational Facilities Authority 

[2:0:66,64] The California Educational Facilities Authority (CEFA) provides qualified nonprofit higher education institutions with assistance 

[2:1:66,74] through a tax-exempt revenue bond program to reduce the costs of financing academic facilities. CEFA may also issue bonds, 

[2:2:66,84] notes, and other forms of indebtedness for student loans to support students' costs of higher education. 

[3:0:66,102] CEFA consists of the following five members: the Director of Finance, the State Controller, the State Treasurer, and two public 

[3:1:66,112] members appointed by the Governor. 

[4:0:66,143] 3-YEAR EXPENDITURES AND POSITIONS

[12:0:66,595] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[12:1:66,613] 0940 - BOND FINANCING 

[13:0:66,631] CEFA issues revenue bonds to assist private educational institutions of higher learning construct educational facilities. Because 

[13:1:66,641] it is authorized to issue tax-exempt bonds, the Authority may be able to provide more favorable financing terms than might 

[13:2:66,651] otherwise be obtainable. CEFA also may include qualifying nonprofit entities as eligible program participants for the 

[13:3:66,662] construction of student and faculty housing. Bonds issued by CEFA are not a debt, liability, or claim on the faith and credit or 

[13:4:66,672] the taxing power of the State of California or any of its political subdivisions. The full faith and credit of the participating 

[13:5:66,682] institution is normally pledged to the payment of the bonds. Bonds issued for this purpose are not subject to the state's "private 

[13:6:66,692] activity" bond ceiling as specified in federal law.

[2:0:133,38] 0989   California Educational Facilities Authority - Continued 

[3:0:66,65] For purposes of the California Educational Facilities Act, "private college" or "private participating college" means a private 

[3:1:66,75] college that does not restrict the admission of a student based on his or her race or ethnicity, provided that the financing does 

[3:2:66,85] not violate constitutional provisions. 

[4:0:66,103] "Bond" means bonds, notes, debentures, securities, or other evidences of indebtedness. This broad definition allows CEFA to 

[4:1:66,113] issue tax-exempt direct or private placement loans for colleges and universities, which may provide greater cost savings than 

[4:2:66,123] these colleges and universities might otherwise realize on a traditional bond financing. 

[4:3:66,141] 0955 - COLLEGE ACCESS TAX CREDIT FUND 

[5:0:66,159] Chapter 367, Statutes of 2014, required CEFA to administer the College Access Tax Credit Fund and allocate and certify the 

[5:1:66,169] tax credits for taxable years beginning on or after January 1, 2014, and before January 1, 2017. Chapter 22, Statutes of 2015, 

[5:2:66,179] added a requirement that CEFA continue to allocate and certify the tax credits for taxable years beginning on or after January 

[5:3:66,189] 1, 2017, and before January 1, 2018. Chapter 527, Statutes of 2017, extended CEFA's responsibility to administer the tax 

[5:4:66,200] credits for taxable years beginning on or after January 1, 2017, and before January 1, 2023.
